THE HAMMER OF THE SUN



Your Vulcanverse Adventure Starts Here

Vulcanverse is an open-world solo role-playing game. You can play the books in any order, starting anywhere you like, moving to other books whenever you travel to the region they cover. Instead of a single storyline, there are virtually unlimited adventures.

To play all you need is two dice, an eraser and a pencil. You only need one book to start, but by collecting other books in the series you can explore more of the Vulcanverse.

If you have already adventured using other books in the series, you will know your entry point into this book. Turn to that section now.

If this is your first Vulcanverse book, read the rest of the rules before starting at section **1**. You will keep the same adventuring persona throughout the books - starting out as a novice but gradually gaining in power, wealth and experience throughout the series.

**ADVENTURE SHEET**

The Adventure Sheet lists everything you'll need to keep track of while playing. Don't fill it in yet. That will happen as you begin your adventure.

**Attributes**

You have four attributes which typically range from -1 to +2 as you're starting out. You will discover your attribute scores as you play. They are:

Charm: Your understanding of people and their motives.

Grace: How agile, supple and quick you are.

Ingenuity: Cunning and reasoning, and your ability to think on your feet.

Strength: Physical might and endurance.

The maximum possible innate score in an attribute is +5. If you are already at maximum and are told to add to your score, it has no effect.

**Items that augment attributes**

There are items you can acquire during your adventure that boost your attributes while you have them. These are:

Charm: **laurel wreath** (+1) and **golden lyre** (+2)

Grace: **recurve bow** (+1) and **winged sandals** (+2)

Ingenuity: **hornbook** (+1) and **abacus** (+2)

Strength: **hardwood club** (+1) and **iron spear** (+2)

You can only use the bonus from one such item at a time. So if you had a **laurel wreath** that gives Charm +1 and a **golden lyre** that gives Charm +2, you'd only get the Charm bonus from the latter. Similarly, two **laurel wreaths** still only give you a +1.

An item can augment your attribute score above the innate limit of +5. If you have a Strength score of +5 and you possess an **iron spear**, your total Strength bonus when making a roll counts as +7.

**Making an Attribute Roll**

Attribute rolls are made to see if you succeed at a task. These are rolls of two dice with a difficulty that you must equal or beat to succeed.

For instance, you might be told: 'Make a Strength roll at difficulty 7'. You roll two dice, add your Strength score (including the modifiers for any one possession that boosts Strength) and to succeed you need to get 7 or more.

_Example: You are at the bottom of a cliff. To climb it you need to make a Grace roll at difficulty 5. You roll two dice and score 4. Your Grace attribute is -1 but luckily you have_ **_winged sandals_** _which give a +2 Grace bonus, so your modified Grace is +1, which is just enough to make the roll a success._

A roll of double 6 ('boxcars') is always a success regardless of difficulty. A roll of double 1 ('snake eyes') is always a failure regardless of modifiers.

**WOUNDS**

The Adventure Sheet has a box labelled Wound. This is unticked at the start of the adventure. From time to time you may be asked to put a tick in it. You only have one Wound tick at a time; if you're asked to tick the box when it is already ticked, you don't add another.

While the Wound box is ticked you have injuries, and these will cause you to deduct 1 from any attribute roll until the box is unticked.

**SCARS**

You begin with no scars, but may acquire them from lasting injuries or from returning from the afterlife. Scars are a mixed blessing. Many people will shun you because of them, but others will admire or fear you more.

**POSSESSIONS**

Possessions are always marked in bold text, like this: **iron spear**. If you come across an item marked like this you can pick it up and add it to your list of possessions.

You can carry up to twenty possessions at a time. If you come across an item you want when already at your limit, you'll have to discard something to make room. There are places in the Vulcanverse where you can leave possessions and come back for them later.

**MONEY**

You can carry any sum of money (measured in a currency called pyr). You'll discover as you play whether you have any money to start off with.

**GLORY**

Glory starts at 0 but will grow as you perform deeds that increase your renown. With high Glory you will be recognized as a hero and given more respect by those you meet.

**CODEWORDS**

There is a list of codewords. Sometimes you will be told you have acquired a codeword. When this happens, put a tick in the box next to that codeword. If you later lose the codeword, erase the tick.

The codewords are arranged alphabetically for each book in the series. In this book, for example, all codewords begin with O. This makes it easy to check if you picked up a codeword from a book you played previously. For instance, you might be asked if you have picked up a codeword in a book you have already adventured in. The letter of that codeword will tell you which book to check (eg if it begins with N, it is from Book 1: _The Houses of the Dead_).

**TITLES**

Titles record the achievements you have earned, marking you as the champion of a city, protector of a temple, admiral of a fleet, or even a monarch. You will be told when you acquire a title.

**BLESSINGS**

If you fail an attribute roll, you can use up a blessing to roll the dice again. You can only do that once per roll, so you cannot use a second blessing to get another reroll if the first one fails.

You can have up to three blessings at a time. You start the adventure with no blessings. Usually the place to get blessings is at a shrine or temple, but you may find other opportunities to acquire them.

**COMPANION**

As you travel the Vulcanverse, you will meet some people who are willing to journey with you. You can have one companion at a time. When you pick up a new companion you must remove your current companion, if any, from the Companion box. You can also part company with a companion at any time just by deleting their name from the box. You do not have a companion at the start of the adventure.

**CURRENT LOCATION**

You'll use the Current Location box from time to time to keep track of where you are. You will be told when to use it. Whenever you are told to record an entry number in your Current Location box, first delete any number that was already there.

**NOTES**

Use the Notes box on your Adventure Sheet to keep a record of quests, clues or other things you need to remember.

That's all you need to know to get started on your adventure. Now turn to [**1**](#_1)**.**

#### 1

In your waking moments you rarely think of your early life. The world is harsh, drawing a stark line between the quick and the dead, and so there is little time for idle reverie.

But when you sleep and your soul becomes unpinned from your body, time is a river that can flow both ways. Sometimes you see yourself then as an infant, standing on the hard-packed floor of your home as various uncles and aunts come with gifts for your birthday.

There is a man whose face is twisted by a scar that runs across his right eye, a sightless milky orb. He holds out a hand from which an old sword-cut sheared several fingers. Your uncle Nicomachus.

'No, little one, come to me,' calls your aunt Terpe from across the room, singing a little song that makes you laugh in delight.

Your uncle Sophos has a gift for you. 'See these pictures of distant lands,' he says, holding up a book whose coloured pictures blaze as if with inner light. 'When you are a little older you will read the words that describe these lands and travel there in your imagination.'

In the doorway waits your aunt Eremia. She never joins the throng at the hearth, always has one foot outside. 'Imagination's a fine thing. Better still to travel there in person.'

It is as though this moment has happened already. The four wait, and then you go to one of them. But which?

Scary uncle Nicomachus goto [**59**](#HeadingNum59)

Friendly aunt Terpe goto [**592**](#HeadingNum592)

Clever uncle Sophos goto [**634**](#HeadingNum634)

Restless aunt Eremia goto [**597**](#HeadingNum597)

#### 2

As you leave the arena, one of the King's guards accosts you. 'Hail, gladiator. His majesty has granted you a signal honour. You are to be appointed to guard the palace.'

'Me? In the praetorian guard?'

He shakes his head. 'Not quite that. You'll be part of the night watch.'

'Can I think about it?'

He laughs. 'Of course not. It's a royal decree. Come with me.'

You are marched to the palace and assigned to patrol the throne room. Outside, the daylight bleeds from the sky and night enfolds the city under a wing of darkness perfect for furtive deeds.

goto [**350**](#HeadingNum350)

#### 3

It seems surprised that you are still alive. 'You're a hardy one. A hero, perhaps. But you'd best rely on luck and main force in future, for you are no rival in wits to Odysseus, nor even Polyphemus for that matter. Now begone. I am weary and long to return to the realm of dreams.'

It nestles its huge head back on the pillow of sand. As you walk off it adds in a sleepy murmur, 'Never seek this place again, hero, or it will go ill with you.'

goto [**210**](#HeadingNum210)

#### 4

You walk along the east bank of the river. With the irrigation channels of Tethys restored, fertility has been brought back to the land. But where formerly you could walk across the sand-clogged channel, now a mighty torrent surges by. If you want to travel west you'll need to find a way to cross.

Cross to the west bank goto [**834**](#HeadingNum834)

Journey to the Cataract of Tethys goto [**1320**](#HeadingNum1320)

#### 5

'That just doesn't look right to me,' says the younger fisherman. 'The way her mouth is tilted up, it's like she's expecting some juice to drip from whatever she's eating.'

'Honey cakes can get runny,' says the father. 'Especially in hot weather.'

'Yeah, but you'd just shove a cake in your gob, you wouldn't dangle it like that. That says fruit to me. And the way her teeth are delicately parted, see. She's about to nip off a grape, not bite a chunk of basbousa.'

You retrieve the **honey cake** while they go on critiquing the precise intention of the sculptor.

If you have **blessed fruit** goto [**917**](#HeadingNum917)

Otherwise you may as well go back up to the clifftop goto [**421**](#HeadingNum421)

#### 6

You swim out to the island. The water is bracingly cool and fresh. Subterranean streams from all across the west of Notus must drain into this lake.

The island is crescent-shaped with a shore of coarse silt in which you notice paw-prints and a few fish skeletons.

Try fishing in the lake (if you have a **gladiator's net**) goto [**842**](#HeadingNum842)

Explore the island goto [**997**](#HeadingNum997)

Climb back up the well goto [**32**](#HeadingNum32)

#### 7

Several men of the village are lounging on the benches here passing around a pitcher of ale as they watch their wives and children working in the fields.

'So, an intriguing stranger,' says one as you enter the lesche.

'A far-ranging traveller.'

'A mighty hero.'

'We have need of one of those,' says a burly fellow whom you take to be the headman from the way the rest defer to him. He offers you the pitcher. 'What do you say? Will you solve a little problem for us? There's a cyclops up in the hills who keeps stealing our livestock.'

'Well, chickens,' puts in another man. 'In fact, mostly he snaffles eggs.'

'Once he carried off a lamb,' insists the headman. 'Anyway, he's a thorn in our side and why should he get fat on our hard work? We need somebody to go up the hill and sort him out.'

'All right.' goto [**1631**](#HeadingNum1631)

'Some other time.' goto [**466**](#HeadingNum466)

#### 8

Do you have the title **Favoured by Orion**?

Yes goto [**601**](#HeadingNum601)

No goto [**1218**](#HeadingNum1218)

#### 9

Your first thought on waking is that you don't remember going to sleep. Your second thought is that perhaps you're still dreaming, because you are being tugged about but you have no sensation in your limbs. You can't even move.

With an immense effort you peel your eyes open. Stapes is bending over you holding a raw red bone. Is that for the cooking pot? It looks too big, almost like a human arm.

He moves the arm, shaving away bits of flesh with a sharp hunter's knife, and as he does so you roll over. It's your arm. He's skinning you alive.

'All be over soon,' he says. 'You should be a good fit for me.' And as he says that he pulls off the skin covering his skull and tries your face on for size.

And now you know it is not a dream. The stew was drugged. A wave of dizziness rises darkly around your vision and mercifully you spiral down into oblivion.

goto [**143**](#HeadingNum143) and tick the box there before reading on.

#### 13

In this delta stands the mighty city of Iskandria, risen again with the return of the life-giving waters of the Potamegalos. Here the Myrmidons live, a warrior race who fought for Achilles in the Trojan wars.

Once more Iskandria teems with life, commerce, arts, and crafts. Ships ply the Great River; its banks are home to farms and fisheries, vineyards and breweries for the making of fine wines and barley beer. Irrigation canals extend from the Great River into the deserts, creating farmlands and oases to feed the people of the city. After long centuries in which it lay dormant like a dried seed, the land is again blessed by the gods and filled with abundance.

Crops and trade have brought the people of the delta from far and wide. Long scattered, now they are gathering to rebuild the city and restore its fortunes. The old avenues are cleared and lined with palm trees. The marble palaces are repaired with baked brick. Pennants fly above the rebuilt walls. Colour, commerce and noise have brought Iskandria back to life.

Pray at the temples goto [**1470**](#HeadingNum1470)

Shop at the market goto [**689**](#HeadingNum689)

Go to the library goto [**964**](#HeadingNum964)

Go to the north gate goto [**783**](#HeadingNum783)

Visit the tower of Pandora goto [**1635**](#HeadingNum1635)

Stay at your townhouse (if you have the codeword **_Ozone_**) goto [**683**](#HeadingNum683)

Leave the city goto [**850**](#HeadingNum850)

#### 15

The debating chamber is a semicircular amphitheatre where the senators debate matters of administration, diplomacy and law. They listen politely to your suggestions and afterwards the Princeps accompanies you to the door with his hand on your shoulder.

'We are always interested to hear your thoughts,' he says. 'The Myrmidon people can never forget all you've done for us.'

#### 17

Blessings are free of charge if you are a worshipper of Athena or have title **Saviour of Iskandria** or the codeword **_Ozone_**. Failing any of those you must pay 10 pyr per blessing.

You can have a total of three blessings at a time. Each blessing can be used to roll again when you fail an attribute check (Charm, Grace, Ingenuity or Strength) but only once in any given situation - so if you fail the reroll, you can't spend another blessing to try again.

Enter the inner sanctum goto [**270**](#HeadingNum270)

Leave goto [**916**](#HeadingNum916)

#### 18

The latrine is in an outbuilding, below which lies a cesspit that provides manure for the fields. The **moon pearls** go out with you and they don't come back. Cross them off your Adventure Sheet.

goto [**436**](#HeadingNum436)

#### 19

Just as the figure draws level with you, you are momentarily dazzled by a shaft of sunlight. You see that it is not the one you thought, but a stranger.

'Good day to you,' the stranger says, nodding as you pass.

goto [**1555**](#HeadingNum1555)

#### 20

Get the codeword **_Oasis_**. Lose the **roll of canvas**, which you've used to repair the sails, but keep the **celestial tarbrush** as that seems to endlessly replenish itself.

It takes a day or two to patch up the vessel. 'You're going to need a crew,' says a man who has come to watch you work.

'A small two-masted schooner? I could manage her with half a dozen hands.'

He puts his fingers to his lips and gives a piercing whistle, and quite soon five salty-looking men have come to join hm.

'We're the original crew,' admits the first man. 'When our boat got wrecked here, we were so disheartened that we took up a lubberly life of fishing and odd jobs. But now we're all hankering to get back out on the waves, if you'll have us.'

You shake each of them by hand. 'Come on, then. Let's get her in the water.'

If Galatea is with you goto [**478**](#HeadingNum478)

If Polymnia is with you and you do not have an **astrarium** goto [**1315**](#HeadingNum1315)

Otherwise goto [**1063**](#HeadingNum1063)

#### 21

Over the doorway is a plaque commemorating Loutro. 'He kept alive the mysteries of his goddess's cult when all others had lost faith,' says a pilgrim who is ritually washing his feet before entering the temple.

A priest comes outside. 'And it was Loutro who restored Tethys's bounty to this parched land,' he says, with a sweeping gesture that encompasses the lake and the orchards and fields around it. 'He called back her spirit in the form of the Great River that waters the world.'

What will you say?

'No he didn't. That was me.' goto [**659**](#HeadingNum659)

'I know Loutro, and he's everything you say.' goto [**1256**](#HeadingNum1256)

#### 22

You give Psyche the **electrum key** (cross it off your possessions) but even as you start to explain she gives a yelp of delight that causes everyone in the library to look up in shock.

'It's the key of our palace!' she says, trying to remember to keep her voice to a whisper. 'I must go at once. He will be waiting for me.'

As she's stuffing things into her bag, she glances back at the papyrus on her desk. 'Too bad I'll never get to finish that mathematical proof, but love is beyond algebra.'

You accompany her to the library steps and watch her hurry away across the plaza.

goto [**409**](#HeadingNum409)

#### 23

'You do get about,' says Galatea, speaking as if she just saw you a few minutes ago.

'I thought you might be here.'

'I don't know what it is that keeps drawing me back to this spot.'

'Fond memories?'

She thinks about it for a moment. 'I like thresholds. They're neither one thing nor the other.'

'There's a lot of things out there you could say that about.'

'I should travel more,' she says dreamily. 'You miss out on so many experiences when you're a statue.'

If you have fewer than 5 scars, or if you have the codeword **_Quail_**, then you can make Galatea your companion. (If you currently have a companion you'll have to say goodbye to them by deleting them from your Adventure Sheet.)

Decide where you'll go next:

Into the city goto [**1594**](#HeadingNum1594)

Into the desert goto [**1310**](#HeadingNum1310)

#### 25

Twin wedges of darkness stretch leisurely across the width of the tunnel from behind, blocking your way. They are wings whose jet-black feathers are as glossy as obsidian knives. You turn and find yourself face to face with the goddess of vengeance.

'Is it divine aid you use to see your way?' she asks. 'That's forbidden to mortals.'

'Divine? No, I - '

'The underworld is a lightless place. Like this.'

She brushes her fingers across your face and a veil seems to fall like cobwebs.

'You'll just be blind for a short time,' says Nemesis. 'Very short, if you aren't careful.'

With a rustle of wings, she departs leaving you alone in the dark. goto [**722**](#HeadingNum722)

#### 26

Early the next morning, before the stonemason and his family are awake, you take the dog up into the hills. She is very old, with half-blind rheumy eyes, and after a growl that is more quizzical than fierce she meekly allows you to pick her up.

'What is this?' rages the cyclops, hefting his heavy axe with its blade of sharp black flint. 'Faithless rogue! Oath breaker! You swore to bring the daughter. Now the Furies will harry you to your dying day and beyond.'

You stand your ground. 'I swore to bring you the one who replaced you - this poor old mutt. The daughter wasn't born for another ten years.'

'Has it been so long?'

'She was a puppy then. Now see, she is close to the end of her days.'

His twisted face crumples from rage to defeat. Almost without thinking, perhaps recognizing another helpless creature, he reaches out to stroke the old dog's head.

You have a feeling the dog's life will not be so bad. Nor the cyclops's either, perhaps, now that he has a companion in his solitude.

'Remember your vow too,' you say in parting, 'and nevermore go down to wreak harm on the farmers below.'

You return to the village and are welcomed with cheers. 'You have rid us of the monster!' says the headman, thrusting a goblet of wine into your hand. 'Truly the gods smiled on us when they sent you up to our valley.'

Now goto [**983**](#HeadingNum983) and tick one of the boxes there before reading on.

#### 27

The noise turns out to be nothing but a large spider, dislodging small pebbles as it crawls among cracks in the rocks seeking shelter from the sun. It might make a passable supper if you're not fussy - reminiscent of a crab in shape, if not in succulence or flavour.

If Galatea is with you and this box [TICKBOX] is empty, tick it and goto [**982**](#HeadingNum982)

Otherwise goto [**1451**](#HeadingNum1451)

#### 28

The senior umpire raises her hand. A scrap of cloth, red as a splash of blood, flutters on the hot noon breeze. 'Ride out to that rock,' she says, indicating a landmark almost a kilometre away. 'Contestants must turn on the far side. Deliberate collisions will be disqualified.'

She lets go of the cloth. Whips are raised. Reins are braced. As the red cloth touches the dust, you're off.

Make an Ingenuity roll at a difficulty of 10. That is, roll two six-sided dice and add your Ingenuity, trying to score 10 or more. (For this contest you cannot use the bonus of any items you possess except for a **solar horseshoe**, which gives +1 to the roll.)

Succeed goto [**619**](#HeadingNum619)

Fail goto [**794**](#HeadingNum794)

#### 29

'Gynoids.'

'Not androids?'

'Strictly speaking androids are constructs with a masculine appearance.'

'That certainly doesn't describe you.'

Perhaps there is the ghost of a smile. 'I am not an artificial construct.'

'Oh. I thought Vulcan made you.'

'Hephaestus. But he used divine power, not mere technology, and then each of the Olympians endowed me with a spark of their essence. I am as much a living person as you.'

'But there are artificial women?'

'Hephaestus - Vulcan, as you call him - has made many. Androids too. I suppose he enjoys the challenge of fabricating them out of metal rather than working a miracle. Also there was a mortal sculptor who fashioned an ivory statue he called Galatea. He fell in love with her and Aphrodite gave him sacred fruit whose taste brought Galatea to life, but when the sculptor grew old and died Galatea lost her wits to grief and chose to become a statue again.'

'A sad tale.'

'All love stories end that way.' She rises. 'Well, I have things I must attend to. I will let you find your own way out.'

goto [**13**](#HeadingNum13)

#### 31

A thousand empty miles lie silent all around. Even the great limestone mesas of the Land From Which None Returns are over the horizon behind you. What peoples tramped across this terrain, now covered with the dust of dead aeons?

Do you press on, though there is nothing in sight? If so goto [**243**](#HeadingNum243)

#### 33

You are awarded the title **Champion of the Amazons**. Note it on your Adventure Sheet.

'But I'm not an Amazon,' you say as the Queen puts the trophy into your hands.

'Our will and our actions are what define us, not mere accident of birth. In spirit you are one of us.'

goto [**1265**](#HeadingNum1265)

#### 34

Again you turn towards the pyramid and set off doggedly across the dunes. Your water has run out and there are no oases, not even cacti that you could break open for a little moisture. Your lips feel like old cracked leather. A strip of cloth across your eyes doesn't stop the sun from blistering your skin.

Day by day, the pyramid inches nearer. At night, slumped under the entombing roof of night, you see it as a pale companion to the moon. But as you finally think it is within reach - just another few hours - the horizon spins like a plate and the pyramid is snatched away. You stagger, dizzied by the sensation of movement, and when you recover your balance the pyramid is back in the far distance, a mere speck on the lip of the world.

If you have any blessings then cross them off. They are gone, stripped away by this accursed place that is abandoned even by the gods.

If Polymnia is your companion goto [**918**](#HeadingNum918)

If you are with Galatea goto [**232**](#HeadingNum232)

If you are with Chipos goto [**1183**](#HeadingNum1183)

If you talk to Loutro goto [**1285**](#HeadingNum1285)

Otherwise you can set off towards the pyramid (goto [**220**](#HeadingNum220)) or give up and try to get out of the desert while you still can (goto [**904**](#HeadingNum904) ).

#### 35

As the last daylight drains out of the sky, you catch sight of a knot of phosphorescent white seeping from behind some rocks. Faint voices mutter from beyond a crack in the cliff. It is the dead, mustering to emerge into the lands of the living.

Some small boulders and tree branches are sufficient to seal the fissure.

Back in the village, the farmers are aghast at your appearance. 'Have you wrestled with Thanatos himself?' asks one who also serves as priest. 'Been mauled by Orcus? Inhaled the breath of dread Erebus?'

'It's nothing that rest and a good meal won't cure,' you say, trying to assure yourself more than them, for the taste of the mists is in your mouth now, and you are not sure you'll ever be truly free of it.

goto [**983**](#HeadingNum983) and tick one of the boxes there before reading on.

#### 36

From this vantage point you can gaze out at four of the regions of the known world: the verdant meadows and woods of Arcadia to the north, the sun-scoured plains of Notus to the south, the blue vastness of the eastern sea, and even in the far west, with the aid of a little imagination, the smoke-veiled towers of Vulcan City itself.

Pick the direction you'll go in next.

#### 39

Before long you meet a physician with a staff and a bag of herbs. He gazes at you with a mild, myopic expression. 'Greetings to you,' he says. Then he notices the obsidian blade in your hand.

'It's not personal.'

'It feels a little personal.' He sighs and sits down on his pack by the side of the road. 'One of those mad religious things, I suppose.'

'Ares demands your death. Luck of the draw, really.'

'I heal soldiers on the battlefield,' he says, holding up a roll of bandages as if it was documentary proof. 'Isn't that a good thing, from the standpoint of an Ares-worshipper?'

Enough talk. Kill him goto [**1141**](#HeadingNum1141)

Let him live goto [**1247**](#HeadingNum1247)

#### 41

By the time you could find your way back across the Styx, re-enter the living world, and get back here, the skin that Malleus took off your corpse before will have dried out and rotted beyond recognition.

#### 42

#### 45

'A picnic! A picnic!' sing the children, surrounding you.

You open your basket, which is indeed a hamper full of fruit juice, sandwiches, ice cream, sweets, pies, and buns along with plates and cups and party hats.

The children eagerly devour the lot, boisterously shouting and shoving each other, treading in the jellies, smearing chocolate down their faces, and having a snowball fight with the cream buns.

'Nothing left,' says a little girl, looking into the hamper which you now see is much larger than you thought. She claps her hands and the other children immediately stop what they're doing and burrow into the sand, wriggling until they have all disappeared.

You set off, dragging the basket which you know you can't leave to litter the desert. After a while you see an old man in the distance. He seems to be struggling with a heavy burden and you could easily catch him up if you didn't have the basket to hold you back.

#### 51

The cockatrice rears up, turning its head to bring its killing gaze full upon you. The air shimmers and darkens in a fetid green wave. As its eyes come to rest on you, a tightness grips your throat and burning pain sears through every nerve.

You turn to run, stumbling. There's a thud and now you are sprawled headlong in the dust. You didn't even feel the impact of the fall - your limbs are already numb with approaching death.

The world falls away and you are left with the image of that appalling glare burning in the darkness.

#### 52

The streamers of snow that pour off the higher peaks tell you the wind's speed and strength. Running over, you finish it off with another rock. Strange - its blood is not so red as its plumage.

But as for its plumage - ! Those richly coloured feathers should fetch a good price in the markets of the south. You pluck the carcass and can add 1000 **phoenix feathers** to your possessions.

There is nothing to keep you here so you set off down the mountainside. But something troubles you. Why do you find yourself hurrying on with nervous backward glances as though you had committed a heinous crime?

#### 56

The chariots thunder around the track, kicking up thick clouds of dust and jolting with bone-crunching force against each other as they constantly vie for pole position. Instead of leather whips, the contestants wield lengths of chain with which they knock splinters of bone off any horse that isn't pulling its weight.

Roll two dice to determine who wins. On a score of 6, 7 or 8 it's Talus. On any other roll Ilium comes in first.

If you have the title **Champion of the Amazons** you have experience in judging a skilled charioteer and can nudge the dice roll by 1 in either direction.

If your choice lost the race, you forfeit the amount you bet. If you picked the winner, you get back your initial stake plus 75% (if you backed Ilium) or plus 125% (if you backed Talus).

You adjust fractions in the bookies' favour. For example, suppose you had put 10 pyr on Ilium to win and he did. You would get back 10 pyr (your original bet) plus winnings of 7 pyr (75% of 10, rounded down).

#### 69

Pygmalion was a sculptor of such consummate skill that visitors to his workshop often took his statues to be living people and would start a conversation with them until they noticed that the other stood unmoving.

'These are not statues,' said a philosopher who visited Pygmalion's studio one day. 'These are frozen moments of life.'

Reading further, you find an account of his greatest creation, a woman whom he called Galatea. She was so beautiful that poems were written praising her and flowers were left for her at Pygmalion's door.

One day a very ugly man came to the sculptor. 'I have fallen in love with Galatea,' he said. 'She is the only woman who looks back at me without disgust.'

'She has only one flaw,' said Pygmalion, 'yet that is a considerable one. She is not alive.'

The man pressed a fat purse of gold into his hand. 'I give you all I have for one night with her. Leave me here tonight, and I will be gone by morning.'

And so he was - but so too was the statue. And since then many have sung of Galatea's beauty, but no one living has ever set eyes on her. At least, that is what the books say.

#### 79

You're climbing a ravine towards a rock face that disappears into a low bank of cloud. It's a strange feeling to stand here looking up at the underside of a cloud only thirty metres over your head. It makes you feel that if you went much higher you'd have to crouch under the roof of the sky.

A figure emerges from the cloud, making his way sure-footedly down the near-vertical slope above you. He wears a traveller's hat with a floppy brim and carries a long staff. 'Greetings,' he says, not seeming even to look at you. His voice echoes off the sides of the ravine.

'Good morning,' you reply warily.

He continues to survey the length of the ravine while addressing you. 'Perhaps you have seen one I seek?'

'Hard to say. What does this one look like?'

'You might know her by her ways. She is good with children and animals, though when I think of her it's always surrounded by herbs and flowers.'

You indicate the bare grey rocks. 'Few flowers here, fewer animals, and no children.'

'She might be in the valley.'

'Does she have a house there?'

'Someone might have taken her in. She has a knack for finding friends among those who dwell down on the ground.'

'On the ground?'

'Far away from the clouds, that is.'

He looks up. You follow his gaze. The white sky seems to stir like milk, there's a dizzying feeling of falling -

And you sit up. You must have taken a nap and dreamt it all. As the sun is declining in the sky, you hurry back down the mountainside so as not to get overtaken by the night.

#### 93

'Ah, to that irresistible force even the god of love is not immune. He fell for a mortal girl, and took her to a palace where they lived for a short time in bliss. But his mother Aphrodite was jealous and arranged things so that the lovers became estranged. Oh, the sweet pain of it. You see why Eros's powers are characterized as barbed arrows that cause agony if they're withdrawn.'

'Why was his mother so against the match?'

'Partly because Psyche's beauty was so great that many compared her to Aphrodite herself. But there was something else strange about it. After telling Eros to make Psyche fall in love with a hideous monster, Aphrodite is said to have given him a long open-mouthed kiss. Now, those Olympians have some peculiar ways, but that's odd even for them.'

'And what became of them?'

'Perhaps the story isn't over, you mean? I'm not going to talk about the future, but Psyche went back to her books - she was always a studious girl - and Eros no doubt returned to wreaking all sorts of delicious mayhem. Love makes the world go round, they say, but sometimes it brings it to a crashing halt too.'

goto [**369**](#HeadingNum369)

#### 113

'I come bearing a message from your god.'

You strain to move your head to look, but your face remains pressed into sticky blood-caked sand. You have a memory of intense pain, of a knife whittling you down to muscles and sinews and finally to the raw bare bone, but now you have no feeling at all.

Something pecks at your cheek, making a hollow clattering sound, and your head rolls round to see a vulture squatting beside you.

You groan. 'That makes sense.'

You don't hear the sound of your own voice, but the vulture seems to understand you. 'Oh, there's a bit of cartilage he missed.'

The bird's beak darts down to peck a bit of flesh from your bones.

'That's not funny.'

'Nothing funny about you either. Where are your gambols? Your songs? Your flashes of merriment? You should see yourself. Lipless. Eyeless. Ossicles are not comical.'

'I should be dead.'

'Picture me raising an eyebrow,' says the vulture. 'You are dead. That's what the god's message is about. Do you want to be thrust back into life the usual way, or would you rather be raised as a skeleton?'

'Trick question, is it?'

'A skeleton would blend right in among the Spartoi. But don't let me sway you. It's your decision. And the risk is that you're on your own. If you get killed in undead form, you're dead forever.''

'Then make me a living skeleton.' goto [**1702**](#HeadingNum1702)

'Bring me back to life properly.' goto [**476**](#HeadingNum476)

#### 161

Do the gods speak as mortals do? Demeter's voice is like the nearness of thunder - not because it is loud, but because it makes the world shake and each syllable reminds you that in the face of eternity your life is but a flicker. So the eagle's shriek must sound to the vole. You hear what she says not simply in your ears but because it burns itself like fresh memory into your brain.

'My daughter Persephone has been carried off,' she says. 'Forbidden by Zeus to search for her, I fled Olympus and came here in the guise of the girl Kybele. You were kind enough to take me in, so I suckled your son. He has supped on the very nectar that feeds young gods. Having grown fond of him, I decided tonight to burn away the mortal chaff that poisons his spirit, so that he could become a demigod.'

'We are not worthy, O Goddess,' says the Queen, her voice muffled because she now has her face pressed to the floor.

#### 170

'Come out!' you call, but they are whispering and giggling to themselves inside the tent and take no notice.

You know what will get them out. Stoking the firewood, you put the lit stove under the opening of the tent. The canvas strains as hot air fills it up. Soon it's rising off the ground, swollen to a nearly spherical shape. You forget about the teenagers in your hurry to attach the basket before the balloon takes off.

It drifts over the desert, whose dunes look like ridges of grey dust and where the runnels between them are as dark as ink. But as you survey the horizon you are dazzled by a shining pyramid. Its surface is encrusted with diamonds that reflect the moonlight in sharp shimmering fragments.

Your stove is out of fuel. As the balloon descends, the Sphinx looks up from its nest atop the pyramid and you see its gigantic hooked beak, jagged crest of feathers, and stare of predatory intent.

The thundering of bony hooves, the rattling of chariot wheels, the crack of chain whips, the harsh curses and shouted boasts, the clang as the contestants swerve against each other - all evoke a curiously hollow and funereal reflection of a chariot race among the living. Roll two dice to determine who wins. On a score of 2 to 7 it's Vomer. On a roll of 8 or more it's Ilium.

If you have the title **Champion of the Amazons** then you are an expert judge of each contestant's form and can nudge the dice roll by 1 in either direction.

If the charioteer you backed lost the race, you forfeit the amount you bet. If you picked the winner, you get back your initial stake plus 70% (if you backed Vomer) or plus 130% (if you backed Ilium).

You adjust fractions in the bookies' favour. For example, suppose you had put 12 pyr on Ilium to win and he did. You would get back 12 pyr (your original bet) plus winnings of 15 pyr (130% of 12, rounded down).

#### 192

The fifty-metre outer walls of the arena are tiled with travertine that sparkles palely in the noon sun - a dazzling contrast to the sombre black stone of the rest of the city.

You enter through one of several great arches, each four times the height of a man and decorated with faded and peeling stucco reliefs. A short flight of steps brings you out on a balcony that leads to the spectator area.

Banks of seats climb in row after row around the sweeping sides of a cracked and half-fallen but still impressive amphitheatre. Here the skeletons sit eagerly awaiting the next fight to the death. In the middle is a circular arena at least a hundred paces across. Tunnels at the sides, sealed with massive bronze gates, must be where the gladiators and animals enter.

The royal throne is on a wide platform set close to the arena's edge, just a few metres above the floor of packed sand where combats are fought to the death. The King obviously likes to get a good view. The seating gets less impressive the further it is from the ringside. Right at the top, the highest tier of seats is shaded from the sun by an awning of stitched hides - recognizably human from the flaccid eyeless faces, dangling empty fingers, and clumps of dead dry hair.

#### 214

The streets leading into the market square are so packed with people that nobody can move. It's a surreal sight: hundreds of panicked citizens wedged shoulder to shoulder, faces contorted with fear, all carrying whatever belongings they were able to salvage - yet shuffling along no faster than when they're cramming into the temples on holy days.

Over their heads, on the far side of the market, you can see the raiders ransacking the stalls. They are carrying off bales of silk, sacks of grain, wriggling livestock, jars of wine, and armfuls of silver and copper coins.

Some of the trapped refugees follow your gaze. 'Look,' says one. 'They're putting slave collars on those they've caught.'

He's right. Some of the raiders have taken over a blacksmith's stall and are dragging captives there to chain them.

#### 215

The Spartoi, or sown men, spring up when a dragon's teeth are planted in the ground. For this reason they hunt dragons, but are careful to protect dragon's eggs to ensure the species does not go extinct. The end of dragonkind would also spell the end of the Spartoi race.

Several authors describe the Spartoi as skeletons, but a few claim they have stiff, weather-beaten features and bodies covered in old scars. The explanation of this paradox seems to lie in a gruesome custom among the Spartoi. They are skeletons, but when they catch a living person they skin them and stitch their preserved flesh into a garment that the skeleton puts on like a suit of clothes. Wearing a stolen skin earns the skeleton status so that it is accorded great honour by other Spartoi - until the skin rots and falls off.

goto [**116**](#HeadingNum116)

#### 216

#### 220

The pyramid is there in red darkness, like a coruscating slice burned into your retina, inescapable, eternal, terrible to behold.

A million steps towards it gets you no closer. A million more. You try to wake from fever only to find the pyramid hovering before you again, brighter than the sun. On its crest sits a creature with the unpitying stare of a raptor. It opens its beak and you realize you are its prey -

You sit up with a cry. You're on a blanket in a nomad's tent. The canvas ripples in a faint dusk wind, and even that cool breeze stings your blistered skin. A moist rag is touched delicately to your lips.

'You're awake,' says a voice. 'We found you crawling across the dunes ranting of bird-headed gods and seas of treasure.'

The ordeal has taken its toll. Lose 1 from Strength (even if that takes your Strength modifier negative) and if you had any companion except Loutro then cross them off, as they have deserted you.

After a few days among the nomads you feel ready to press on.

#### 223

There is no opening behind you. It is as though you simply stepped out of empty space. Or perhaps you hallucinated the whole experience? Yet you touch your belongings and they still feel damp and chill from the subterranean air.

#### 245

In its heyday the hippodrome must have presented a magnificent sight. Three tiers of stone benches rise to a height of over twenty metres around a central oval race-track six hundred metres long.

There is room for over a hundred thousand spectators, though the Spartoi are not nearly so numerous as that. The sparse groups of onlookers, little huddles of bone dwarfed by the grandeur of their surroundings, only serve to accentuate the vast emptiness of the now-ruinous structure.

Chariot races are held here throughout the afternoon. The chariots themselves are battered bronze tubs spotted with green patches of corrosion and the horses, like the drivers, are animated skeletons.

The champions of past races are a fearsome sight with missing bones and shattered skulls. You see one hulking brute with ebony prosthetics replacing half the bones in his body. Another has lost the whole top of his skull from the bridge of the nose up. It doesn't seem to affect his control of the chariot, though.

You shut the hamper, which is just as full as ever. She's right. Instead of children, you're surrounded by a lot of over-stuffed pink piglets who are lying on their backs and waving their legs in the air.

'I'll bring a smaller hamper next time,' you say.

'Just a basket would do, dear. A little goes a long way with toddlers. See that you bring a tinderbox too, in case it's anyone's birthday and we have to light candles. And perhaps something to use as a tablecloth.'

#### 277

Filigree panels of dark green jade are set into the inner windows of the palace. They filter the moonlight into a tracery of sea-deep shadows that are perfect for stealth. At the end of a long gallery overlooking the city, you see the silver-studded doors of the throne room.

You steal inside. Flaring lanterns cast a dancing light across the walls, which are decorated with grisly trophies - faces peeled from foes whom the Spartoi considered especially worthy. They hang in eternal expressions of dismay or hate, gazing sightlessly on their conquerors.

Crossing the throne room, you see your goal: the battle standards seized across centuries of warfare. They stand in a row behind the empty basalt throne. Each bears as its crest, fashioned of gold or copper, the head of an animal sacred to the nation who once carried it into battle.

'All are symbols of lost empires,' says a voice behind you. 'Look upon their works now.'

It is the King. He comes towards you, a naked skeleton taller than any other of his people but disdainful of the preserved human skins his nobles wear. One thing marks him out from an ordinary Spartoi warrior: the obscene red glistening bulb of his heart throbbing inside his ribcage.

'I've come too far to let you stop me now,' you say.

If you have the codeword **_Ooze_** and you do not have **_Offer_** goto [**959**](#HeadingNum959)

#### 278

An unshaven middle-aged man is standing under the bare timbers of the roof. He seems lost in thought until he hears your footsteps crunch on the gravel outside.

'Can I still get a blessing here?' you ask him.

He shakes his head sadly. 'The shrine has been abandoned. It would need to be reconsecrated.'

'Are you a priest?'

'Of Aphrodite? No. Though I respect all the gods, of course, there is one who lives in my heart. I'm Loutro, by the way.'

You shake hands. 'Which god is that - the one you worship?'

'One who is almost forgotten these days. I am the last initiate of Tethys, goddess of rivers. In a parched land like Notus there are few who make offerings to her.'

Loutro offers to accompany you for a while. You can note him as your companion if you accept, but you will need to say goodbye to any current companion if so, as you can only have one at a time.

Its hooves raise puffs of dust from the grassland. It's picking up speed. As the sound of its pounding gait reaches your ears, it lowers its head. You realize that it was barely trotting before. Now it's coming at you like a falling boulder.

#### 287

The jeers of the crowd are deafening in the hard echoing walls of the arena. They jump to their feet, each holding his or her thumb down. You've slain their champion and now they want your blood.

You are seized by the King's soldiers and dragged to a guardroom at the back of the palace. There they truss you up and start to work you over, knobbly bone knuckles thudding again and again into your body. After a while the pain becomes a rising red tide that washes away all sensation. All you can feel is the impact of blow after blow that sends you swaying in your bonds. They're not even bothering to ask any questions, just softening you up for a leisurely death.

Tick the Wound box on your sheet if it was not ticked already. At a lull in the beating, as the guards stand around sneering, you manage to gasp:

'Kill me... and have done with it...'

'Eh? What's that? Kill you? That's not where we're going with this. First we're going to tenderize that firm flesh, then we'll lop off your limbs to use as spare parts. We're going to keep you alive, just the head and torso, so you can wriggle around the palace forever like a pet maggot.'

You need to buy some time, recover your breath and look for a way to escape. Perhaps if you were to give up Hyperenor as an accomplice? It's an unworthy thought, but now that it has occurred to you it's impossible to drive it away.

You can neutralize the poison with **cockatrice** **blood** if you have any. (You only need one drop, so don't delete it from your sheet if so.)

'Why don't you jump in there, then? Get yourself some of that luck.'

#### 336

He swings his pack onto his back and walks a few paces before looking back and saying, 'I'll tell you one thing, your lordship. You won't catch me putting on airs and snubbing my old friends, even if I could get myself snug inside the supplest bit of skin this side of Adonis's peachy-smooth arse. So good evening to you, my lord.'

You watch him trudge away sulkily towards the declining sun. It will be dark in an hour, so you press on in search of a good spot to camp overnight.

'I'll tell you a story of bygone times. The civilization of Iskandria was at its height. The city's ships carried goods from the four quarters of the world. Its philosophers studied the heavens and the earth and wrested secrets out of nature that even the gods had forgotten. Its armies marched to battle with proud songs, won great victories, enforced peace. The traveller could see Iskandia's towers and hanging gardens from miles off, and the impregnable walls that enclosed the city. And atop its north gate blazed the symbol of its glory: a giant replica of the famous seal of the Myrmidon dynasty, granted to them by Poseidon himself. Any edict stamped with that seal had the force almost of Olympian command.'

#### 365

You break the mortar seal and swing open the doors. Cool shadows fill the vaulted interior of the temple, which is carved directly from the red rock of the cliffs.

Dominating the hall is the monstrous effigy of the lost god, which has not been carved merely as sitting on a throne, as the idols of most deities are. Instead he is a gigantic stone figure bent double at the waist, with his back and arms extending across the ceiling as though holding up the weight of the rock.

Could it be Atlas, supporting the sky on his shoulders? But Atlas is rarely worshipped except in high mountain regions. And when you look on the god's face you cannot suppress a shudder. His features are frozen in such a look of fury and fear.

There is an archway in the wall between his legs. Steps lead down to another chamber behind and below this one. As you go nearer, you see that it is filled with roiling white mist.

'He hasn't bothered anyone lately, has he? That's all I can say.'

Cyamites nods, respecting secrets as only children can. Warming to you, he gradually confides what he knows about the strange mist. 'It comes down the mountain after sunset. And I saw my mother in it, and other people who used to live around here.'

You heard that Cyamites's mother died last year. 'What do they want?'

He scuffs his foot against a table-leg, embarrassed. 'They want to see us. The people they left behind. But they daren't come too close because their touch is really, really cold. So they hang around outside in the fields watching the houses till the sun comes up.'

It makes sense. The Asphodel Fields are the mist-draped regions where the souls of the dead linger. A breach in the boundary would let the mist out along with some of the ghosts. And their icy presence would certainly blight the crops.

You propose leaving an offering of hens' blood and grain for the ghosts to feed on. 'That will keep them away from the crops until the harvest comes in,' you tell the farmers.

Your plan works and you are again feted as the saviour of the valley. If there's one thing peasants admire more than heroism it's guile.

Now goto [**983**](#HeadingNum983) and tick one of the boxes there before reading on.

#### 393

The sun's blaze feels as if it's magnified here under a sky as clear and unforgiving as polished crystal. Water is hard to find and soon your lips are parched and cracked, your eyes assailed by fine grit.

Will you travel by day? goto [**1076**](#HeadingNum1076)

Or by night? goto [**1674**](#HeadingNum1674)

#### 395

'This woman is my sister,' you say, putting a protective arm around her. 'We have just arrived in the city, so it isn't possible that one of the raiders is the father of her child. While we commiserate with - '

'You lie!' screams one of the older women. 'I've lived next door to this slut for years. She's always been a wrong 'un.'

She lunges at the girl, who breaks away and runs for it. Baying like mad dogs, the others chase after her. You watch them from the temple steps, snatching up chunks of broken masonry to throw at the girl as she runs. On the far side of the plaza she stumbles and falls. The crowd is on her before she can get up again.

Later, as you pray at the altar, you see some of the older women returning. Seeing you glance over, one of them leers at you and says in a horrible cackle, 'She won't have to worry about having a baby out of wedlock anymore.'

Sickened by the way adversity has brought out the worst in these people, you go outside to breathe fresh air. On the far side of the plaza, a puddle of blood shows dark against the marble flagstones. There is no sign of the girl.

goto [**1007**](#HeadingNum1007)

#### 396

The old lighthouse had fallen into ruin, but now that it is needed again the locals have patched it up. A hunter going home with some rabbits he's caught on the downs comes over to chat.

'Couldn't you set a brazier burning up there?'

'A brazier's not really bright enough. Needs a light you can see for miles off, even in fog. Anyway, who'd tend it?'

#### 397

'If you had to choose between a giraffe and a snake, wouldn't you say it'd be better being a giraffe?'

'Dust in your mouth, wriggling on your belly. Nobody has a good word for you. And the poison must make everything taste sour. Life as a snake can't be any fun. I'd rather get a mouthful of green leaves and feel the fresh breeze round my ears.'

Not for the first time, you wonder if she's brilliant or crazy or both.

#### 403

You stalk the hand throughout a long, blistering hot afternoon. You are soon weary and pouring with sweat, but the hand remains fresh and sprightly, sometimes dancing to and fro on a boulder to goad you.

It gets careless - as you planned. Darting too close, it is taken by surprise by your sudden burst of energy. The net is cast. The hand is snared!

Furiously it writhes in your grip. It's stronger than you thought. It breaks free and dashes for the cover of the rocks, no longer so insouciantly mocking. As it disappears from sight in a crevice, it turns and salutes you.

Caught in the net you find one of its nails, which must have been torn off in the struggle. It is long, graceful, elegantly manicured. The kind of nail a musician grows to pluck at the strings of an instrument. Note that you now have the **Orphean nail**.

goto [**1451**](#HeadingNum1451)

#### 404

Pandora stands looking out over the city from the top of her tower. This high vantage point transforms the smoking rubble and queues of starving citizens into a scene of timeless tragedy that you might see in an art gallery. But you have come up from the streets where the stink and squalor and misery are all too real.

'All of the contents of the box of ills got emptied out down there,' you say, joining her at the parapet.

'It was more of an amphora than a box.'

'And the rest of the story?'

'Oh yes, that's all true. Envy. Rapacity. Violence. Destruction.' She gestures at the broken walls, the burnt and shattered buildings, the huddled homeless in the streets.

'Too bad, just when the old place was coming back to life.'

'It's a chapter break, not the end.' She looks at you. 'True story: after Hephaestus created and animated me, he pretty much lost interest. You know the kind - solving the problem is the challenge, finishing it off is the chore. Luckily he had an assistant who made it a point of pride not to leave a job half-done. His name is Kedalion. He was the one who installed the virtues that the gods sent to form my character. Hephaestus made the framework, but it was Kedalion who brought me to life.'

'That's what Iskandria needs - bringing back to life, only on an industrial scale.'

'Exactly.' She reaches out and prods you in the chest.

'Why do I get the feeling I've talked myself into something?'

'Kedalion will have the means to repair all this devastation. You've just got to find him. Then you can restore the city to its former glory.'

'So where is he? In Vulcan's workshop?'

'No. He struck out on his own. You'll find him among a people called the Galomoi. And before you ask - that's all I know.'

Get the codeword **_Onerous_** and goto [**1007**](#HeadingNum1007)

#### 407

'He must be a fast runner.'

The Sphinx is often said to be inscrutable, but its expression as it looks down at you is decidedly unnerving. You feel as a disfavoured toy might if plucked from the nursery by a scornful child. The huge face presses down close to you and a rush of hot breath makes you shudder.

'It was a he, not a she?' purrs the Sphinx.

'I think so.'

'And they ran north? Or south? Or...?'

'I think - I mean, I surely saw them, heading that way.'

The Sphinx studies the far horizon. 'Not even a speck in sight. Can mortals travel that fast?' It draws in a deep breath. 'Let's find out.'

It roars, and a storm hits full force. Scouring sand is flung into your eyes. Gain 1 scar as you are whipped off your feet and spun over and over like a leaf in a gale. You catch one glimpse of the pyramid, now a terrifying distance below as the Sphinx's bellow sends you far aloft. Any prayer you utter is snatched away by the shrieking wind.

#### 410

'I noticed your herb garden back there.'

'I can't hardly pick 'em fast enough before they spring up again. Rub that one. Smell it? Curry, that is. Can't normally grow that in this climate.'

'Quite a lot of poppies, though.'

He comes down the ladder and looks along the path, which is bordered on each side by sprays of white poppies. 'Yeah, pretty things but weeds as far as I'm concerned. I keep pulling them up, but there's always more.'

The barking has by now turned into a very threatening snarl. It's coming from the hedge behind the orchard. 'Your dog is securely chained up, is he?'

'Lelaps? He's a cantankerous old devil, but his bark's worse than his bite. Oh, hang on now. Watch this, you'll see something.'

The wetnurse, Kybele, has come out with a pitcher for water. She sets off down the path and turns behind the hedge, where there is presumably a well or cistern. The moment she draws level with where the dog is tethered, he stops barking.

'Meek as a lamb with her,' says Klademos, 'but he'll have anyone else's hand off.'

'I thought you said his bark was worse than his bite.'

'So it is, but you heard him, didn't you? His bark would scare the pants off a Myrmidon.'

#### 435

#### 441

The thundering of bony hooves, the rattling of chariot wheels, the crack of chain whips, the harsh curses and shouted boasts, the clang as the contestants swerve against each other - all evoke a curiously hollow and funereal reflection of a chariot race among the living. Roll two dice to determine who wins. On a score of 2 to 7 it's Talus. On a roll of 8 or more it's Vomer.

If you have the title **Champion of the Amazons** then you are an expert judge of each contestant's form and can nudge the dice roll by 1 in either direction.

If the charioteer you backed lost the race, you forfeit the amount you bet. If you picked the winner, you get back your initial stake plus 70% (if you backed Talus) or plus 130% (if you backed Vomer).

You adjust fractions in the bookies' favour. For example, suppose you had put 12 pyr on Vomer to win and he did. You would get back 12 pyr (your original bet) plus winnings of 15 pyr (130% of 12, rounded down).

Stay for the next race goto [**1474**](#HeadingNum1474)

You've seen enough goto [**141**](#HeadingNum141)

#### 450

A hopeless ploy among a tribe of warriors with sharpened combat reflexes. The dagger glints in the sun as you fling it aside. A tall Amazon leaps from the crowd and catches it, in the way that bridesmaids in tamer cultures jump for bridal bouquets.

#### 461

'Surprising how often that goes along with love. They're not even opposites, are they? The opposite of love is indifference, while hate is love turned monstrous. I'll give you an example. The Bacchantes were the priestesses of Dionysos, and how they loved the musician Orpheus. Groupies, I suppose you'd call them, shoving each other aside to tear off a scrap of his clothes, hanging around outside his house at night, that sort of thing. But it all went sour when he turned his back on Dionysos and started worshipping the sun. Or was it that he lost interest in women and started hanging around with pretty boys instead?'

'These Bacchantes. Quite judgemental, by the sound of it.'

'Also known as the Raving Ones, so yes, I'd say a tiny bit unbalanced. Used to cover themselves in tattoos of webs and oak leaves and dress in fox skins. Savage chic, you might call it. Anyway, they ambushed Orpheus at a concert and tore him apart. Not just the clothing this time, they pulled him limb from limb. Tore off his head, chopped off his hands, stamped his guts into a pulp. As for his testicles, they were found with bite marks. Now that's hate.'

'What happened to his body?'

'The head floated downriver and out to sea, still singing. His lyre was set among the stars by one of the Muses. The other Muses buried the rest of him at the foot of Mount Olympus - all except the right hand, that was never found. Eaten by a nutty Bacchans, probably.'

#### 482

The wetnurse reveals herself as the goddess Demeter, Bringer of Plenty. Even though she is dimming her divine aura, she is almost too terrible to look upon. You feel as if you were a drop of spray about to be engulfed by the infinite ocean.

'I came to the mortal world to seek my daughter, who has been taken from me,' she tells the King and Queen. 'You were kind enough to give me shelter from those who are hunting for me. In return, I suckled your son.'

'Before you arrived we thought he was too weak to live...' The King barely seems to know what's going on. He speaks as if talking to himself.

'He would have sickened,' says Demeter, 'but he grew strong on my ambrosial milk. And tonight I was going to burn away his mortality and make him a demigod, for I had grown fond of him and I wished to repay you for your hospitality.'

The King stares into the flames, which are mercifully too bright even to make out the tiny calcined bones that are burning there.

'It is unfortunate that I was interrupted, but console yourselves, mortals. He would have died soon in any case.'

'Soon?' The King looks round, dazed rather than angry. 'But you said he was healthy.'

'He was. Seventy or eighty years, that's soon. An eye blinks, and to the gods a mortal lifespan is gone.'

King Keleos puts his arm around the Queen. She is desolated, bereft of all reason, struggling for breath in ragged gulps.

'And you will have other children,' says Demeter. 'I myself have seven.'

There is a rush of wind, a scent of poppies, an iridescent flash behind your eyelids - and only mortals stand here now. The goddess has departed.

'A helpless child on a mountainside. Listen to her snivelling. Must do your heart good to think that was you twenty years ago. And now here you are. You survived. And you get to mete out the violent end that you so narrowly avoided yourself back then.'

'I was only a baby! They left me here to die!'

'True. Still, you were much stronger than her, weren't you, or you wouldn't be here. And your father is the sky, hers is only a puny mortal. Anyway, get on with it.'

'What?'

'Dash her brains out on a rock. Pull her arms off. Eat her. Whatever it is you cyclopes do.'

He stares back as if it was his own fate you were talking about. With a sob he pushes the child back into your arms. 'Take her home.'

'And you?'

'I'll go. I'll find another mountain. It's torture to stay in this district where I look down and see my parents' home every day.'

'Look up instead,' you call after him as he stumbles away, more forlorn than fierce now. 'Maybe your father really is the sky.'

You take the girl back home and return to a hero's welcome in the village. 'I wonder what will become of the cyclops?' you ask the headman later.

'Who cares? We're well shot of him, that's all that matters.'

Now goto [**983**](#HeadingNum983) and tick one of the boxes there before reading on.

#### 501

Pausanias leads you up the hillside, pointing out fragments of mosaic paving and toppled marble pillars, until you stand at the highest point. From here it's still possible to make out the faint traces of the streets, mere outlines of deeper shading between the silt, rubble, beachgrass and pampas.

The sea wind whistles past you here, and Pausanias points to the wide bay to the south where the sun casts handfuls of diamonds against the turquoise shimmer.

'Quite a view from up here. This was the great plaza. On one side the temples, on the other the royal palace and its ministries.'

'Don't tell me. A constant power struggle?'

He shakes his head. 'You might think so, but no. They left such squabbling for lesser races. Between the priesthood and the court, here in the centre of the plaza where we are now, stood a fountain atop which was mounted the sacred standard that Poseidon gave to the first Myrmidons. As long as the fountain flowed, improbably high up here as it was, both factions were reminded that they shared a common ancestry and must bend their efforts to the good of the whole nation.'

#### 502

'Which temple?'

'Oh, you can't miss it. Wait there at midnight. If anyone comes by, hide under the water till they've gone.'

#### 504

At first I thought it might be Zeus, but he's more about thunderbolt-chucking than cunning. So I met up with Daedalus, and he told me the titans are planning an escape from Tartarus.'

'Well, that's serious.'

'But wait. It gets gnarlier. The gods and titans are just pawns, and the real power here is Nyx, daughter of Chaos. She has sent her sons Death and Sleep out into the world sowing the seeds of her nefarious plan.'

'Which is?'

'To sweep away both gods and titans, seize control of Vulcan's devices that have shaped this new universe, and plunge us all into eternal night in which she will reign supreme.'

'It's never anything nice, is it, where nefarious plans are involved?'

He replaces his mask. 'I'd better get back. Watch your step, friend. I'll get another message to you if I can.'

#### 546

You find what you are looking for in the works of an unnamed poet:

'Niobe was beauteous and blessed, but instead of thanking the gods for her fortune she was proud and boastful. She knew the bitter taste of tears when her twelve children-six lovely daughters and six stalwart sons-were all slain in her house. Apollo killed the sons with arrows from his shining bow and Artemis slew the daughters, because Niobe had compared herself with the goddess Leto. She scorned Leto for only having borne two children, whereas she had herself borne many-and so the two gods came and killed the many.

'Nine days the dead lay in the dust, and none dared to bury them; but on the tenth day the immortals saw that Niobe was worn out with weeping, so they made the ground to swallow the bodies and they turned the grieving mother to stone and set her deep under the earth at the headwaters of the Styx so that she could behold through all eternity the infernal realm into which her own hubris had cast her beloved children. The rock still weeps, and the touch of those tears is enough to cause a person to become afflicted with the same grief Niobe felt.

'_She stands, her own sad monument of woe;_

'_The rock eternal lasts, the tears for ever flow._'

'OK, that's a little more information than I think we needed.'

'Sorry we can't help you with that seal,' say the other fingers.

'We just left it as foothills in the end. Nobody complained.'

#### 575

Towards evening the sky goes black and a thunderstorm rolls in from out at sea. Torrential rain crashes down, extinguishing the burning rafters and sending torrents of mingled blood and ashes flooding along the gutters. Each lightning stroke captures the devastation in a too-clear instant of blinding white, then the darkness drops again and the sky booms its horror at the ruined shell that was once proud Iskandria.

Through the downpour it is just possible to see the raiders' warships thinning out as they weigh anchor and move out under oar.

'Low in the water,' remarks a merchant bitterly, surveying the departing ships. 'Fuckers. Hope the storm sinks them. That's our goods weighing them down.'

'Which way are they heading?' you ask. 'Can you tell?'

He peers into the murk. 'Hard to say. Looked like straight out to sea, but there's no harbour there.'

'They're sailing back to hell!' shrieks a wild-eyed priest clutching a bloody rag to one eye. 'From the abyss of night they came, and that's where they're bound now.'

You help out with tending the sick, organizing food rations and putting up some makeshift shelters, but after a few days you are sick at heart at what has befallen the city. It is time to move on.

goto [**1032**](#HeadingNum1032)

#### 595

You dive through just ahead of the horde of skeletal warriors and the grating clangs shut. In the narrow tunnel the enraged baying of the Spartoi echoes like the howls of Tartarus.

'I've locked it behind us,' says Hyperenor as he leads you along the tunnel. After the bright sunlight of the arena it's as black as night.

'You saved my life.'

'I'd say we're even, but in the custom of my people I owe a debt that cannot be repaid. No, not that way. That leads to the lions.'

He shows you a narrow crawlway off the main tunnel. 'The sewers?' you say dubiously.

'You'll be fine. They're not like sewers in a living city.'

You shake his hand, for once remembering not to grimace at the touch of bare bone. 'Till we meet again, my friend.'

'Don't hurry back. They'll rebuild the Ossuary ten times stronger and meaner. And next time I might not be here to haul you out.'

You squeeze along the narrow concrete shaft. In the stories told about epic heroes, sewer pipes usually are much bigger than this. At least the sewers of Ostopolis are free of excrement, but you have to crawl in claustrophobic gloom for hours until you finally pull yourself out onto a hillside well away from the city walls.

All around are heaps of ancient coins, intricately fashioned jewels, gold cups and plates, bowls of alabaster rimmed with silver thread, rings of precious minerals, all with an inner fire that has been pent-up unseen in the darkness as mountains moved and rivers dried.

Moving with infinite care so as not to disturb the piles of treasure and send them clattering to the floor, you scoop up five **lava gems**. They are much lighter and easier to carry than coins. Add them to your possessions.

For a heart-stopping moment the Sphinx's breath catches. It stirs in its sleep, flinging out a limb that crunches to rest in a pile of rubies. You back away and tiptoe up the tunnel.

Go north goto [**1555**](#HeadingNum1555)

Go south goto [**164**](#HeadingNum164)

#### 636

Roll two dice to determine who wins. On a score of 6, 7 or 8 it's Vomer. On any other roll Talus comes in first.

If you have the title **Champion of the Amazons** you have experience in judging a skilled charioteer and can nudge the dice roll by 1 in either direction.

If your choice lost the race, you forfeit the amount you bet. If you picked the winner, you get back your initial stake plus 75% (if you backed Talus) or plus 125% (if you backed Vomer).

You adjust fractions in the bookies' favour. For example, suppose you had put 10 pyr on Talus to win and he did. You would get back 10 pyr (your original bet) plus winnings of 7 pyr (75% of 10, rounded down).

#### 674

The Sphinx considers you for a long moment, one of those moments when the Fates pause in their weaving and decide whether to snip the thread.

It decides. 'Enter, then. I have a challenge for such a hero.'

It leads the way down into a vast chamber deep under the pyramid. Piles of gems and gold and jewellery lie like debris under the portico along one wall.

'Wonderful things,' says the Sphinx, surveying its hoard with satisfaction. 'Not only for their fiery beauty, like the lights of heaven caught in metal and crystal, but because I like to lie on them and dream golden dreams.'

'And because they bring adventurers here.'

It smiles a carnivore's smile. 'There is that too. Well, you must know the tradition. A riddling game with your life as the stakes.'

'Go ahead.' goto [**864**](#HeadingNum864)

#### 675

Does he mean himself?

He shakes his head. 'You will know the one to give that last grape to, when you meet them.'

You thank him for his counsel and go on your way.

#### 681

You find Keleos and Metanira standing to receive their courtiers. On the throne sits the baby prince, merrily building a ziggurat out of coloured wooden blocks.

Prince Demophon smashes the toy ziggurat and stares around triumphantly. At once the courtiers stop what they're doing and applaud. 'Bravo, Your Highness! Another foe vanquished!'

You notice that the dog, Lelaps, is curled up on the other throne. When the nanny tries to fetch Prince Demophon for his nap, the dog growls until she gives up and goes away again.

#### 685

You stand atop the cliffs at the southernmost tip of the land. From here the ocean that encircles the world extends to infinity. With slow swelling it seems to draw a titan's breath, slapping the shore with waves of pounding foam. The very air exhilarates you with the sense of unbridled energy.

The sea itself comprises layer upon layer. The topmost, clear, like green air with pinpricks of fire dancing within it. Beneath that a glassy turquoise, then a thin gleaming azure bright as a noonday sky. And under them a profound and limitless indigo that swallows everything, even light.

#### 686

The arch is not stone, but formed from the crossed tusks of some truly gigantic ancient beast. Whatever it was, in life it would have been big enough to swallow an elephant whole.

#### 694

After a hard-bitten journey over leagues of sun-baked sand, you come to the heart of the Dunes of Doom - a massive crater in the desert. Some say it was caused by the king of monsters, Typhon - or at least a part of him - falling to earth after a great battle with Zeus, the king of the gods. Others say it was one of Zeus' thunderbolts gone astray, and still others a lump of magic metal that was hurled from Olympus by Vulcan himself.

Either way, the crater remains unfilled by the windblown desert sands somehow, its floor like pitted glass strewn with shattered rocks. And not just rocks but also the rotting and skeletal remains of many warriors and adventurers who have come here in search of glory or lost treasures. There is even the wreckage of a chariot.

In one wall of the crater there is a corbel-arched door made up of three simple stone blocks around a massive door of solid bronze.

If you have the codeword **_Nephew_** or **_Neophyte_** goto [**1426**](#HeadingNum1426)

#### 695

You smear the basket with glue and leave it wedged among some rocks. What should you use as bait? Food would seem to be of no use to a disembodied hand. Finally you decide to rely on the hand's natural inquisitiveness to investigate any strange item.

You deliberately make camp some distance away. In the evening, as you eat a meagre supper, there's a creaking and rustling, followed by a frenzied thrashing.

You run to the trap. Too late - the hand has already broken free and is scuttling away into the darkness like a big pallid spider.

Delete the **wicker basket**, which the hand has demolished in its escape. Also delete the **pot of glue** you used.

Just as you're about to give it up as a bad job, you notice that one of the hand's nails was torn off in the struggle to escape. You clean off the glue and take a closer look. It is well manicured and shaped like a plectrum, as musicians grow their nails. You now have the **Orphean nail**.

goto [**1451**](#HeadingNum1451)

#### 700

#### 704

No one else volunteers to fight the Ossuary. Now that the King is here, the contests become more interesting. One noble after another steps forward, ghastly in his or her stolen skin, to announce a captive human who they're sponsoring to fight. These contests are between whole-bodied gladiators, their sponsors thereby demonstrating to the whole Spartoi nation that they are wealthy enough to risk forfeiting an intact skin.

The sight of desperate struggle and bloody death soon sickens you. No doubt the victors have been promised freedom, but you have learned by now that the Spartoi code of honour is harsh and ruthless, and you suspect the only freedom to be won on these sands is the freedom from life.

You rise abruptly from your seat. But why?

#### 705

Even as you race across the plaza you can see thick smoke pouring out of the library doors. You enter to a scene of pandemonium. Raiders are whooping as they climb the stacks and throw books to each other. You see texts that have been carefully catalogued and preserved for centuries being flung about, spat in, set on fire or simply ripped in half in contests of strength.

The scholars who normally work here have been gathered into a huddle, stripped of their robes, and are being whipped to make them run from one end of the library to the other for the amusement of their tormentors. One, stumbling on the piles of scattered books, loses her spectacles and as she gropes blindly for them a raider crunches her hand into the glass with his booted foot.

You look around but there is no sign of Psyche. Perhaps that's just as well. If she were here to see what the raiders are doing to her books it would break her heart.

#### 711

#### 725

Stapes straightens from examining your pack. He has the teeth in his hand. 'You could be skinned alive in these parts for making off with this little stash.'
