He makes a curious whispering sigh. 'You reap what you sow.'

He throws the teeth down and they wriggle into the ground like bony maggots. You take a step forward, but Stapes dodges away as small mounds erupt from the sand, sprouting to become a couple of dozen skeletal warriors.

'You're my phyle,' Stapes tells them, and immediately they form up in ranks beside him. There are too many to fight. You turn to run, but hard white hands seize you and force you to the ground at Stapes's feet.

'Too bad,' he says, bending over you with his flensing knife. 'I'd got quite attached to you. In a way I still will be.'

How strange that you didn't notice before the loose flaps of his skin, like an ill-fitting suit, the yellowish dried-out complexion - and even, now that he draws close with the knife, the ants in his tear ducts, that make him seem to weep black drops as he skins you alive.

#### 772

After midnight the street lamps on the main thoroughfares have burned out, giving up the city to the undisputed reign of night. You walk on, enjoying the cool breeze after the heat and crowds of the day. The moon is rising from the waves, and on the streets which are now submerged in darkness there glitter white and luminous specks: the pole of a chariot, a shop awning, a flag, or the gilded turrets of a palace. The jewelled idols on the temple roofs gleam like great diamonds.

Further down the hill, as yet untouched by moonlight, ill-defined houses and shadowed gardens form deeper masses in the gloom, and near the harbourfront the fishermen's nets stretch from one house to another like gigantic bats spreading their wings.

At this hour the grinding of the hydraulic wheels which convey water to the highest storeys of the palaces is no longer heard. The camels, lying ostrich-fashion on their stomachs, doze peacefully in the middle of the terraces. Only the occasional bark of a dog disturbs the city's slumber.

The porters are asleep in the streets on the thresholds of the houses. The shadows of the colossi stretch across the deserted squares. Occasionally from the hilltops the smoke of a still-burning sacrifice escapes through the bronze tiling, and the heavy breeze wafts the odours of aromatics blended with the scent of the sea and the exhalation from the sun-heated walls.

The motionless waves shine around the city, for the moon is spreading her light upon the bay, where flamingos form long rose-coloured lines amid the banks of sand, while further on the salt lagoon shimmers like a piece of silver.

To one side the star-filled indigo vault of heaven sinks into the dustiness of the plains, and in the other direction into the mists of the sea, and on the summit of the hill, the pyramidal cypress trees fringing the temples sway murmuring like the regular waves that beat slowly beneath the ramparts.

'Hang on here for a bit,' says Loutro. Leaving his pack with you for safe-keeping, he sprints off along the first of the roads.

After a while you see him coming back, but before he reaches you he detours over to the middle road and walks away again.

Some time later, you see him cross to the third road and investigate that. Finally he returns and stands a little way off.

As you go to meet him, he steps off the path. 'So that I'm not the first person you meet on the road,' he explains.

'Good thinking. So what did you discover?'

'If you take the first road you'll meet an itinerant physician. He seems a nice old boy. On the middle path there's a robber who chased me with a knife. Him I didn't care for so much.'

'And the third path?'

'An enigma. One who has his face covered and didn't answer me when I spoke. He might be weighed down by vow or obligation of his own.'

Consider which of those three you are most willing to slay, then goto [**1413**](#HeadingNum1413)

#### 785

The cyclops drops a rope and hauls you up. The pain in your dislocated shoulder is like a mule's kick, but you grit your teeth until you are safely out of the crevasse. You lie limp on the cold ground.

The cyclops leans close, fixing you with the stare of his single eye. Drool spills from his deformed mouth and his words sound mushy and roughly formed. 'Well? I kept my promise. Now keep yours.'

'To do what?' you manage to gasp.

'I was born to a family down there in the valley, Endoios the stonemason and his wife Praxilla. They didn't like the look of their infant son, so they brought me up here and left me to die on the hillside. But I didn't die. Why? Because I realize now that my true father was the sky god.'

'All's well that ends well, then.'

That yellow stare widens in rage. 'No! I still want revenge. They abandoned me and then they had a beautiful daughter, who they dote on. I want the one who replaced me. Bring her to me and you will have fulfilled your vow.'

'If I do that, will you leave the villagers' livestock alone?'

'Once I have my revenge I will never again go down and trouble mortal men.'

He turns and stomps away among the rocks, leaving you to recover your strength and limp back down the hill. All the way back you are brooding on your promise. You swore to do as he demands - but to put an innocent girl into the hands of that monster?

No, never! goto [**576**](#HeadingNum576)

You must, it was a sacred vow goto [**700**](#HeadingNum700)

#### 786

goto [**1323**](#HeadingNum1323)

#### 790

#### 791

You can't see the cockatrice, so you're safe from its deadly gaze - but not from the fangs and claws. A screech splits the night. The pounding of heavy taloned feet across the flinty ground. The swish of huge razor-sharp wings. Its breath right in your face makes you gag - but you can't see a thing.

Fighting blind, you have no chance. It occurs to you maybe you should light a lamp, but the creature's stare would strike you dead at once. A heavy blow sends you flying, and then the snap of its beak breaks the string of your life.

#### 798

Your fist explodes against his jaw and he goes limp. He doesn't fall because of the tight press of people all around him.

'That shut him up,' mutters the man next to him, rolling his eyes with gallows humour. 'In the nick of time too. Another few seconds and he'd have given the whole pack of 'em the screaming heebie-jeebies.'

You manage to get them all moving in an orderly queue rather than a struggling rabble. In a few minutes the alleyway is clear and you direct a few stragglers to pull some nearby carts over. With luck the raiders won't think to pursue until the crowd have got well away.

'Where should we go?' a woman asks.

'The gates. There's no getting away by sea. Their ships are blockading the harbour.'

The raiders seem more interested in looting the market stalls than seeking out more victims, so you lead the last few people away to safety - reflecting on whether your prompt action saved the day or was a brutal overreaction. If he survives, that young man will be missing a few teeth.

You can choose to increase your Strength by 1 at the expense of reducing your Charm by 1 (an attribute can go negative). It's up to you. Do you think today's events have hardened you, or did you simply do what had to be done to save lives?

#### 806

The chariots thunder around the track, kicking up thick clouds of dust and jolting with bone-crunching force against each other as they constantly vie for pole position. Instead of leather whips, the contestants wield lengths of chain with which they knock splinters of bone off any horse that isn't pulling its weight.

Roll two dice to determine who wins. On a score of 6, 7 or 8 it's Ilium. On any other roll Vomer comes in first.

If you have the title **Champion of the Amazons** you have experience in judging a skilled charioteer and can nudge the dice roll by 1 in either direction.

If your choice lost the race, you forfeit the amount you bet. If you picked the winner, you get back your initial stake plus 75% (if you backed Vomer) or plus 125% (if you backed Ilium).

You adjust fractions in the bookies' favour. For example, suppose you had put 10 pyr on Vomer to win and he did. You would get back 10 pyr (your original bet) plus winnings of 7 pyr (75% of 10, rounded down).

Stay for another race goto [**1474**](#HeadingNum1474)

#### 808

Two petitioners come to argue a case before you. The first maintains it is wrong to enslave men captured in war, for they are of the same race as Amazons and no mortal should own another.

'Nonsense on four counts,' asserts the other. 'First, because we are Amazons and other tribes are our inferiors. Second because we are women and our sex is superior. Third because those we capture in war deserve no better fate. And fourth because without the revenue earned by selling slaves and collecting ransoms, and the work done by those we keep, our people will dwindle in wealth and numbers.'

'How do slaves pay a ransom?' you ask.

The second petitioner answers. 'Their families sometimes send money to buy their freedom. Others may provide services to the tribe.'

'Oh yes, the fit and handsome ones,' scoffs the other.

'Why not? Without men our tribe would die out. Is that what you want?'

'If that's all that bothers you, I think you'll find there are other ways to attract men's amorousness than to threaten them with servitude.'

Your chamberlain leans close to your ear. 'They'll debate this forever if you let them, Majesty. Have you made your decision?'

What will you say?

#### 811

'The fresh water they carry up for this side of the square is muddier than those over there are getting.' 'Fish stew again? I'm sick of bloody fish. I want lentils.' 'You gave me less than you gave her!'

Fights break out and you have to wade in and stop them. Luckily most of the survivors are either old or injured, and in any case hunger has made them too weak to resist when you wrestle them apart.

'Why don't you piss off back to your own city?' shouts a man who spits in your face. 'We don't need charity from foreigners.' But he still takes the bread ration you've handed him.

She gives a sad smile. 'No, they're all grateful. But they're also scared and they're proud, so all you can see now is resentment. Wait, though. In a year or two, they'll all tell stories about the kind outlander who came to help when they needed it most.'

#### 838

The day wears on. Waves of heat ripple above the sand of the arena. The stench of blood wafts on the air.

Midway through the afternoon, a ripple of lyres and drums announces the arrival of King Incus. He takes his seat on the broad onyx throne directly overlooking the arena floor. Unlike his coterie of nobles, who are all clad in stolen human skins, the King disdains to emulate the living. He wears a crown and cloak but otherwise his bones are bare, polished to a gleaming sheen of whiteness.

One other thing marks him out from all the other Spartoi. Inside his ribcage is a real, red, beating heart.

As a row of battle standards are set up behind the throne, the spoils of past wars in which the Spartoi were victorious, King Incus raises his sceptre. Immediately the chattering dies away and there is silence.

#### 861

The land drops away below the horizon and there is nothing but sky and sea all around. Climbing the rigging, you feel salt spray on your face and revel in the sensation of plunging into an unexplored cerulean realm with no limits.

When the sun is high you are a speck in blue immensity. Nights enfold the ship in a gulf of uttermost darkness flecked with moon-limned foam and starlight. When winds rise you ride through lightning-stoked storm and gunmetal swell. Dawns and dusks mix all the colours of creation on the palette of the heavens.

Your crew are uneasy so far out into the unknown, but they trust that in the clockwork volutions of the astrarium you are able to read a true course back to harbour.

'Listen, then. I have three riddles.'

#### 869

An emissary comes from a town in the hills to protest at the Amazons raiding outlying farmsteads in his province.

'It is the way of our people to seize what we need by force of arms,' you tell him. 'We are powerful women, perpetually at war with all other races.'

'I do not dispute the ferocity and determination of the Amazons,' he replies in mellifluous tones. 'The burned buildings and stolen livestock of our farmers attest to those qualities. Yet how much more favourable it would be to both our peoples if we chose the path of trade instead of war.'

You laugh. 'You would have us become merchants? We are the Amazons!'

He looks down as if in humility. 'Yet you grub between the dunes for a cup of brackish water and a plate of stolen goat stew, while in my town we feast on wine and grapes and roast meats, and sleep on feather quilts. Some, comparing these lifestyles, might take the Amazons for impoverished bandits rather than warriors.'

'How dare you speak thus!' blazes the chamberlain.

He looks up hastily. 'Not I, for I remember the glorious days of Amazonian victories, when you were accounted equal to the Myrmidons in battle. But I remember this only because I have read about it in books.'

The emissary bows and leaves. The generals of your court face you, awaiting your decision:

'But that must have been aeons ago. You mean they're still here?'

'In the early days they were forever laying into each other, seeing as how they hate everything that walks or crawls. But over the years they formed themselves into regiments, appointing their own commanders from those that won a game of knucklebones. They took over the forts that the Myrmidons built to control these lands back in the day. Now the Spartoi range up and down the desert in search of blood. She laughs. 'The poppy? Of course it does. Poppies represent Demeter, goddess of fertility. Very popular at weddings. Oh, that's almost a pun.'

'Only in English.'

'Strictly speaking it should just be the white ones, as the red poppy symbolizes death, but I've got to do what the clientele want.'

#### 903

The obelisk leans at a slight angle, like a clock at one second to the end of time. A few dead clumps of thorny scrub, scorched by the sun until they have become as hard as stone, obscure the pedestal. You hack them away excitedly in case the inscription reveals the regnal name of the First Ancestor, primordial ruler of this land, who is now dimly recalled in stories that are mostly myth.

But you are disappointed. The name, enclosed in the decorative cartouche with which the monument builders of old venerated their kings, is no longer legible. Is it only weathering that has effaced most of the letters - or do you discern the marks of a chisel that deliberately cut away the First Ancestor's name?

#### 904

Near dusk you come across a mortuary temple whose ancient columns stand like broken teeth. The roof has fallen in but a bas-relief of a hawk's head still stares out from the cracked pediment. The floor inside is covered with sand, and in the middle is a catafalque carved with hieroglyphic descriptions of the afterlife.

From the paintings on the walls, half-erased by time but still faintly visible, you gather that a mummified body was laid in state on the catafalque before its final journey to the pyramid that would be its tomb.

Outside the sun sinks into the realm of Hades and the stars leap out exultantly against the canvas of the night.

#### 906

'Kadmos can see this is a tight spot. The Spartoi don't much care for the living, heroes or otherwise. But he notices that they don't all belong to the same phyle.'

'What's that?' you say.

'Phylai - I suppose you could say tribes, or clans, really.'

'I thought you said "phyle"?'

'That's the singular. Anyway, each Spore - '

'That's the singular of Spartoi, is it?'

Stapes nods. 'Depending on whether he comes from an incisor or molar or whatever, a Spore will be of a different phyle. Kadmos, thinking fast, chucks something in among them that they all wanted.'

'What was it?'

'You're a tough audience.' He shrugs. 'I'm not sure. Good point, a story should have all the details. It was some treasure, anyway. Something the Spartoi really prize. And the various phylai start scrapping till only the toughest five warriors are left in one piece. "We are the sown men of the Capitate clan," says one, and as you are wily and have helped defeat our rivals we will serve you."'

'I thought they wanted to kill him?'

'Complex code of honour, the Spartoi. You wouldn't understand it.' Stapes stretches and heaves a silent yawn. 'So they became his loyal bodyguards and he founded the city of Thebes. We better get some sleep, eh? Long hot day ahead.'

goto [**734**](#HeadingNum734)

#### 907

A bony hand clutches your arm. You freeze in shock as it pulls you behind a rock.

'Hyperenor! Good grief, I nearly jumped out of my skin.'

'Then you'd really be one of us,' he says with a rattling laugh. 'Sorry to give you a start. It's not easy going "psst" with no lips.'

'Why the secrecy?'

'Are you all right over there?' shouts Stapes.

'That guy's your problem,' Hyperenor whispers urgently. 'He's not a living man. He's one of my people.'

'A Spore? But he's...'

'Got skin? Yes, he peeled it off a traveller he murdered. That's what we do, wear the skin of a human victim. We get status that way. And now the skin is going off a bit he's planning to do the same to you. So keep your wits about you - and your flesh.'

'I'd better get back before he suspects something. Thanks, Hyperenor.'

#### 910

It was not the way of the heroes of old to tiptoe modestly about the world. You call down into the darkness, declaring your past deeds and the feats with which you yet intend to make your mark.

Time passes. At first the only answer to your defiance is the thin sigh of the wind, lapping the sand against the base of the pyramid.

A gust of cooler air billows up out of the gloom carrying a strong animal reek. That's how you are first aware of it. Then the sound. It makes you think of something huge being dragged across stone. Footfalls like padded boulders. A purring that vibrates your stomach.

A human face looks up the tunnel towards you. A face as big as a ship's hull.

'Who spoke those bold words?' rumbles the Sphinx, playfully extending a paw that could crush a wagon. 'That's one I'd like to meet.'

#### 914

'It took more than three hours to walk the perimeter of the city walls. Those were the outer defences, stone ramparts thicker than ten ranks of fighters arrayed for battle, and so tall that if you stood on the head of an elephant you would still come barely halfway to the battlements. Look there, you can still see the stumps of the great gates to east, west and north.'

It takes some imagination to superimpose the grandeur he's describing onto the vista of barren hillocks and scrub-dotted plains.

'Most magnificent was the gate to the north, called the Tethys Gate, wide enough for six carts to pass through abreast. It was faced in lapis lazuli so that it gleamed like deep blue water. The gate posts were covered in inscriptions marked out in gold, and atop the tower shone Iskandria's seal. Many a traveller, sighting that seal from afar, gave thanks that he was nearly home. The gates were cedarwood, and washed and oiled daily, so that in the dawn you could smell a fragrance of forests in the air.'

'The way you talk about it, it almost sounds like you were there.'

He sighs. 'The poetic licence of the dragoman. But in my imagination I have often passed under that gate and mingled with the many interesting people to be met beside it.'

#### 935

You turn to the pregnant girl. 'And see, dreadful as the calamities you have each faced, her fate is worse still. For the raiders took her and used her for their pleasure, and when the baby is born the natural love of a mother for her child will always be tainted by those terrible memories.'

#### 936

You liken your own plight to Thumb's. 'You and I, we know what it's like to have to soldier on when in pain. If you make a fuss, everyone else gets fed up with you. If you put a brave face on it, they forget the discomfort you're in and don't make any allowance for how hard it is.'

#### 943

A skeletal hand reaches out of the darkness of the tunnel, unlatches the grating and pulls you through. As you race along the tunnel you hear the grating clang shut and Malleus's enraged shouts behind you.

You squeeze along the narrow concrete shaft. In the stories told about epic heroes, sewer pipes are usually much bigger than this. At least the sewers of Ostopolis are free of excrement, but you have to crawl in claustrophobic gloom for hours until you finally pull yourself out on a hillside well away from the city walls.

#### 944

You squelch up onto the shore near the nomad camp. 'They can swim pretty well, you know,' says one. 'You can't escape from them by swimming.'

'Then why didn't it chase me in?'

'All rhinos are short-sighted. They tend to go by sound. When you stopped swimming to look back, it couldn't hear you anymore so it lost interest.'

#### 949

The **phoenix tears**, rubbed into your eyes, give you the ability to see in the dark. You have to use all your supply, so delete them from your list of possessions.

The night seems to magnify sounds: the scrape of your feet on the rocky ground, the mournful howl of a jackal, the heavy slither of a reptilian tail.

The cockatrice appears to you as a bulky serpentine outline of greenish light amid a field of black. Rocks appear dimly, still holding a little of the day's heat. You can see nothing else - but the cockatrice can see nothing at all. Sensing your approach it rears up, standing on powerfully taloned feet and swishing its spike-edged wings. A crest of sharp feathers opens around its head like a fan of knives. But its eyes have no power to harm you in the pitch dark.

If you run in to strike it goto [**1643**](#HeadingNum1643)

If you stand off and rain arrows on it from a distance goto [**151**](#HeadingNum151)

#### 950

Kybele generally sits with her shawl pulled around her and goes about stooping to disguise her height, but as she stands drinking a cup of barley water you can't help noticing that she is a strapping young girl. She must be well over six feet and yet she doesn't seem at all masculine. In fact there is something almost luminously female about her.

'Do you find your duties here arduous?' you ask her.

She turns with a smile like sunrise and answers you graciously. Her accent sounds too aristocratic for a mere servant. And then you realize that she's already moving away and you completely failed to take in what she was saying. It is as though her voice transported you to another realm of being. They say the poets can do that.

There's a little barley water still in the cup, which she has left by the sink.

Drain the cup goto [**755**](#HeadingNum755)

Talk to someone else goto [**1299**](#HeadingNum1299)

#### 952

It's not hard to catch sight of the mist. It rises in the evening and gathers roiling in the narrow passes, faintly luminous in the starlight, then it creeps down the hillside and hangs around the farmstead until dawn.

The hard part is figuring out the source of the mist. It arrives so swiftly that it is already a blanket of whiteness by the time you see it drifting through the dusk. And as the sound of cocks crowing echoes up from the valley, it rolls back so quickly that you can't be sure which of several steep ravines must lead to the fissure.

You push further up the hillside and have to spend several nights out in the open with only the hard rocks for pillows. On the third night you wake to see a pale hand stroking your arm. The touch is no more substantial than a spider's web trailing across your skin. You look up into your father's face. You can see the moonlight right through him.

You don't remember any more until you wake at dawn. The sun barely penetrates the steep-walled pass and you feel as if you have crawled out of a bath of ice. Finding a stream to wash yourself, you are shocked by your reflection. Your face looks haggard and there are new streaks of white in your hair. No one can meet the dead and remain unscathed.

If you were unwounded, tick the Wound box on your sheet. You could not survive another night like that.

Keep searching for the fissure goto [**35**](#HeadingNum35)

Go back down to the village goto [**466**](#HeadingNum466)

#### 956

'Take the stretchers into the undercroft,' orders the high priest. 'Barbarians do not fear the living but they do fear the dead.'

'There are tombs below the temple?' you ask as you help carry the injured down into the cool gloom.

'No. But the barbarians may think there are.'

The work of treating the many casualties keeps you busy all afternoon. More are being carried in all the time, some past any medical help. The undercroft is filled with the stench of blood, sweat, excrement, urine - and the occasional waft of incense that seems to open a door into the worlds beyond from which the gods look down on human suffering.

At last, unable to bear the stifling airless room and the pitiful moans of the wounded, you go up to the main temple. It has been stripped of gold and gems. A few bodies lie across the floor, and at first you think they are praying. But they are too still. From their postures and clothing it's clear the raiders toyed with them for pleasure before they died.

#### 968

The major-domo closes the door and ushers the servants away. Bowing his head again, he says, 'You do, your grace. Welcome home.'

#### 971

The Ossuary prowls out from his lair beneath the arena, stooping as he comes along the tunnel. As he straightens to his full height, do you feel a qualm? He is a patchwork creature assembled from the bones of men, giants and monsters. His face comprises the pelvic girdle of a human or giant ape, the gaping foramina being the eye sockets and the rest of the pelvis a huge blunt crest. The mouth has been taken from the skull of a reptile. The limbs must use the bones of elephants, so thick and hard are they - though you notice he has a third arm consisting of a whole string of forelimbs that curls up from his spine like a scorpion's tail.

He stands almost twice your height, and in each of his three arms he has a cleaver of the kind that butchers use when they need to hack through a bone.

He strides forward. He's saying something, but his voice is shaped by the clacking mouthparts of the crocodilian jaws and it takes you a few moments to understand him.

'You are scrap. You are nothing. I will grind you to powder and the wind will blow you away.'

#### 972

The work-gangs must have come this way under the tyrant's lash, thirsty, brutalized, knowing that after they'd broken their hearts and bones raising the pyramid to the sky, they'd stay out here to dry in the sun and join their bodies to the sands.'

Your companion nods. 'True, that would be worse.'

#### 984

Inspired by this, the next day you pretend to forget the arm, kicking over the ashes of the campfire, shouldering your other belongings, and trudging off leaving it behind.

Instead of following you to strike taunting poses, as it normally does, the hand scurries out of cover and sidles towards the unattended arm. #### 1019

'The ways of the gods are strange indeed, and yet they seem at times almost envious of those whose road is only threescore miles and ten.'

'Which road?'

'I refer to the road of life. To a god it is a track heading off to infinity with no bends or crossroads to mitigate the tedium. Imagine that. Be glad that, as a mortal, your path will bring you at last to the end.'

'You said they're envious. How?'

'The things the gods care about are often mortal pursuits. Hermes, the winged-heeled messenger of the Olympians, is a shepherd who likes nothing better than to laze on a hillside in the afternoon sun. Demeter, mother of plenty, delights in the beauty of a field of poppies. Zeus - he's very fond of mortals, as everyone knows, though only if they're young and comely. Even dread Nemesis is fascinated by mankind; you don't hound somebody to death if you're indifferent to them. Consider how all of the gods must dream of what it means to be earth-born, doomed to grow old and die. To mortals each moment is precious. Each drop of water or bite of an apple is a taste to be savoured. The gods cannot have any appreciation of how it feels to live your whole existence on a razor's edge. You should pity them.'

#### 1043

You gather the militiamen and tell them to bulk up their numbers with the younger men in the crowd. 'Ransack these buildings for furniture we can make into barricades. You others gather all the weapons we can muster. Anything we can fight with, even garden shears and sacrificial daggers.'

They're glad of the orders. Giving them something to do fends off panic. More than that, if you can stop the raiders from getting into the plaza then the city will never have to suffer the disgrace of barbarians rampaging inside its temples and Capitol buildings.

You don't have long to wait. The raiders come in a great howling tide, pouring up the hill from all directions. They have painted their faces with the blood of those they've slaughtered, and some have wound human entrails round their necks as trophies.

The fighting is long, brutal and erases all sense of time. You race from barricade to barricade, helping to shore up defences, directing field dressings for the injured, getting archers up onto the temple roofs to give cover, and at times wading in yourself with a sword in each hand.

It's like fighting hydras. Each time you cut one down, it seems as if two more spring up to take his place. You are slipping in the gore. But wave after wave breaks against the barricades and still they haven't managed to get into the plaza.

Abruptly there's a lull in the fighting. Are they pulling back? A bloodthirsty yelping rises from the raiders' front ranks and some Iskandrian children are dragged through. 'You got one chance,' shouts a barbarian captain, wrenching a child off the ground and laying his sword on her throat. 'Surrender, or these whelps will feed our dogs tonight.'

Faces slack with horror, the defenders look to you for leadership. What order will you give?

'Take down the barricades!' goto [**1511**](#HeadingNum1511)

#### 1057

Drums are pounded to signify the beginning of a bout. In any arena back in the living world it would be trumpets, but trumpets are not among the instruments favoured by the Spartoi.

The contests are drearily monotonous. Living captives are driven out into the arena, usually already wounded, some of them with bandaged stumps where a limb has been hacked off. These poor wretches stand blinking and confused until skeletal lions are released. The crowd hector the victims with shouts, promising them freedom and riches if they survive, but the spark of hope has already left them. They are quickly torn apart by the lions. Soon the sand is soaked in blood, and living slaves - all of them old or crippled - scurry out to sweep it clean.

Occasional contests between gladiators of rival phylai, seeking to avenge a slight or settle a matter of ancient honour, provide some excitement amid the tedious inevitability of slaughter. But such bouts are few and far between.

'Why don't they put in some healthy, uninjured contestants?' you say to your neighbour. 'Then we might get to see a fair fight.'

He's shocked. 'Intact skins are far too valuable to waste in the arena.'

Overhearing this, another spectator leans over. 'Anyway, today might be the day that somebody takes on the Ossuary. Then we'll get our money's worth.'

Wait to see some more fights goto [**838**](#HeadingNum838)

#### 1062

You thought they were house cats, but the creatures that fall ravenously on the fish are lions. Their roaring splits the air as they gleefully devour the **fresh fish** you've tossed down for them. (Cross it off your possessions.)

You now see that it is not a divan the lions are pulling, but a copper chariot studded with emeralds and rubies. And the woman who you took to be an indolent aristocrat is a fierce crowned figure whose aura of power sets your hairs on end.

'As reward for your help, mortal, I stand revealed to you in my true form. I am Rhea, divine guardian of the earth.'

'Then I thank you, goddess, for this beneficent reward,' you reply with a bow.

She considers. 'Perhaps it is little enough, since you have restored my cats to their full vigour. Well then, I shall make you my herald and favour you above all others. And take this little token of my esteem.'

She indicates a set of antique playing pieces lying in the chariot. 'I'm not familiar with the game..?'

'They're for senet. I used to play it with the Sphinx all the time. The handsome one, that is. There should be a board too. I'll look around for that and maybe give it to you next time I see you.'

She gestures and the lions set off. You watch until her chariot is lost in the glare of sun and distance.

#### 1074

The cat suddenly jumps up onto the palace wall, across to the roof, and scampers up over the tiles. A moment later you see him squeeze through a gap in the eaves.

Setting a cat loose in the royal palace - that could spell trouble. You are considering whether to saunter away whistling your most innocent tune when a strange bird comes crashing out of the chimney. Its plumage is a blaze of dazzling white against the blue enamel of the sky and its song sounds like the most delicate wind chimes.

Bathys comes scrambling back over the wall and chases the bird down the valley in the direction of the shrine. It's like watching bagpipes stuffed with butter bounce exuberantly down a hill. You didn't imagine Bathys could be so agile.

By the time you get to the shrine, the old man is sitting up and the priest has fetched a saucer of milk for Bathys. 'A miraculous recovery,' you say.

'It was a caladriole,' says the priest, his eyes shining with wonder. 'I never dreamt I'd see such a legendary bird.'

'It sat on my chest and drew out the fever,' says the old man, seeming stunned to be alive and well. 'Its feathers turned yellow and then it gave a squawk and flew off.'

#### 1075

A thin shaving of moon gives you just enough light. The fanciful rock formations around you assume a nightmarish unreality in its pearlescent glow. Other than the scrape of your feet in the grit, there is no sound.

By sheer chance you come across the impression of a body in the dust. It looks as if someone fell here out of the sky, but there's no sign of them now. Looking around you notice ripples that sweep away from the body's outline to right and left, like the imprint huge wings might make.

Did the man - or woman - fly away? Or did the fall stun or kill them and somebody or something else carried them away? The wilderness is a void into which a thousand such mysteries could vanish and leave no trace.

#### 1079

Strabo's _Geographica_ has what you are looking for, though you almost overlook it because he begins by talking about another tribe entirely:

'The Amazons are nomads of the south-eastern plains but they pass two months of the spring in the neighbouring mountain region, which is the boundary between them and the Halizons. The latter also ascend the mountain according to some ancient custom for the purpose of performing common sacrifices, and of having intercourse with the Amazons, in secret and in darkness, the man with the first woman he meets. The motive is not affection or pleasure but to perpetuate both tribes. The female children that may be born are retained by the Amazons themselves, while the males are taken to the Halizons to be brought up there. In nature the tribes are alike in bellicosity, caring nothing for any other peoples, whom their language does not even characterize as human.'

#### 1081

The nearest head opens its mouth, and you poise to jump willingly into it - and then the other heads dart in, fanged jaws biting at the first neck. Within moments the whole mountainside is shaking as the hydra's heads fight each other for the human morsel in front of them.

You take advantage of the distraction to sneak up to the thick bulb of flesh where the necks join the body. A long shard of flint, chipped from the rocks in the creature's frenzied struggle, lies conveniently at your feet. Sweeping it up, you plunge it into the heart. Dark green blood spurts out. The monster is a long time dying, and its howling gasps and the clash of scales on rock echo from the peaks, but at last it shudders and lies still.

#### 1089

For hours you pore over the fragmentary inscriptions you've collected from the four obelisks, but it is futile. The script in which it was written has not been used in a millennium. And even with copies from all the obelisks, there are still gaps where the carvings were too worn away to be legible.

The sun is setting. Weary after staring hard at the inscriptions, you take a meagre supper and are soon asleep. But your mind, over-stimulated by the labour of the afternoon, will not rest. You imagine yourself floating - no, carried on a litter as servants rush to scatter scented palm leaves ahead of you.

You are not conscious of speaking, but you hear a voice that can only be your own: 'Bring forth the tribute,' and at once a series of chieftains bow before you, bringing caskets overflowing with pearls and rubies and gold.

You raise your arm, on which perches a hawk with fierce eyes like droplets of lightning. As if in recognition of the piles of treasure brought as tribute, the hawk spreads its wings, a creature as imperious as its owner. Again your voice rings out: 'Your offerings are accepted. Now you are subjects of my empire and your energies, previously wasted in warfare, will go to increase my glory and your own prosperity. I bring you the peace that is the gift of absolute command. Hear my decree, which is that of the living god, the first ancestor, Usirmaatre, sovereign of sovereigns.'

As trumpets sound and drums beat, the whirl of colour and movement becomes a blur and you are carried down into a deep sleep from which you awaken at dawn fully refreshed.

#### 1095

Iskandria is now far grander than before it was sacked. Kedalion's automata have restored the city to the peak of its magnificence in ancient times.

Behind its rings of defensive walls and its wide blue harbours where masts tight-furled with sails make a forest of canvas beside the quays, the city rises in many tiers atop the hill. Where buildings of mudbrick and plaster stood previously, now there are tall houses built of stone, often covered with decorative shells, and roofed in blue-lacquered semicircular tiles. The city's many gardens provide splashes of lush greenery amid the houses that blaze white and gold in the sun. High above, seagulls wheel and shriek against the sky.

There are wide plazas linked by boulevards that encircle the hill and countless intersecting lanes, many of them with steps, that cut across them from top to bottom. The enclosures of the old quarters rise here and there like great reefs rebuilt from ruined ramparts, now half-covered with flowers, through the arches of which streets run like rivers under bridges.

Those streets are filled from morning till evening with noisy crowds: traders calling out their wares; peacocks screeching on the temple lawns; young boys shaking little bells at the doors of the public baths; parrots and monkeys chattering in the cypress trees shading the boulevards; the heavy chink of coins on the market trestles. The spice-scented air resounds with the clatter of hooves, the ringing of anvils, the deep murmur of the crowds, the thud of crates unloading on the dock, the booming temple gongs, the ceaseless rumbling of cartwheels. White cockerels, sacred to Apollo, crow on the terraces. The oxen that are being led for sacrifice bellow in the temple groves. Steam and smells of cooking billow out from the food stalls. Sweat, perfume, dung, incense and blossom mingle together in an aroma no native of the city could forget. Slaves run about with baskets on their heads, and in the depths of the porticoes priests and nobles can be seen in resplendent robes, arms dripping with gold.

#### 1114

'There was once a great battle between Typhon, king of monsters, and Zeus, king of the gods. They fought for days, Typhon uprooting mountains and hurling fireballs from his eyes, Zeus throwing thunderbolt after thunderbolt at his foe. Every time Zeus hit his foe, bits of Typhon were blown off, to fall all over the earth. Except that Typhon was said to be so big his head touched the stars, so for a while he endured until one of Zeus' bolts took out his eye. It fell into the desert where it hit with such force, like a rock hurled into a pond, that ripples of sand were blown outward for miles from its landing place. Thus were created the Dunes of Doom.'

#### 1133

On your first night back among the Amazons you notice a brooding silence at dinner but think no more of it until hours later, when you are dragged from your bed, carried out, and flung down on the hard ground outside your tent. A wind has risen in the night, howling and whipping up dust and snapping the canvas flaps of the tents as if the whole world were in the grip of a mad frenzy.

Hundreds of eyes gleam in the guttering torchlight. All the tribe are gathered here to watch. You scan the crowd for Phoebe, your chamberlain, but the faces blur together in the leaping shadows.

'What is this perfidy? I am your Queen!'

'You have forfeited the right to command by your treason against our sacred codes.' One of the nobles, a veteran named Tekmessa, confronts you.

'What the Queen does cannot be treason.'

She snorts. 'You learned nothing of our ways. Our oath is to the Amazon tribe. A monarch rules only as long as she is fit to rule, and you have proved yourself unworthy. By this mark all who see you shall know.'

Before you can get to your feet, you are seized and pinned by several tough warriors. Taking a knife, Tekmessa carves a long slash across your face. Gain 1 scar.

Pressing her lips close to your ear Tekmessa whispers, 'Be glad we are not as unforgiving as the Amazons of yore. Once a deposed Queen would lose her nose and ears before being cast out.'

Almost fainting with pain, you have no strength to resist as they fling you over the back of a horse. You are carried far from the camp and tossed down in the dust.

As they ride off, a voice calls back, 'We're merciful this once, but don't ever return if you want to live.

Lose the titles **Amazonian Queen** and **Champion of the Amazons** and get the codeword **_Omen_**. Then turn to the section number written in the Current Location space on your Adventure Sheet.

#### 1187

You can smell the lake before you see it: a fresh, fecund aroma that makes you think of greenery. As you draw nearer, the dusty scrub gives way to grassland. Trees along the horizon swim in a sultry heat-haze.

The lake is huge. After wandering a continent so full of vistas of sand and rock, emerging from low hills above this vast expanse of water takes your breath away. Millions of pinpoints of light dance on its glittering blue-grey surface. You can see fishing boats, and there are the tents of a nomad encampment.

And the wildlife - it's like seeing a dried flower bloom. You spot a family of elephants stomping and trumpeting in the shallows, gazelles prancing over the savannah, a pride of lions lazing by the shore, flocks of birds drifting overhead. There are even the lumbering grey hulks of a large herd of rhinos that are tearing up roots and shrubs a little way off.

Breathing in the scents of wildlife and vegetation and fresh water, you make your way down to the shore.

#### 1225

Days pass. The pyramid grows nearer. At first you could cover it with the nail of your little finger, then with your thumb. Now it takes on shape - a precise triangle of glaring light that burns and shimmers on the horizon.

A shriek rings out, shrill and exultant - the attacking cry of a bird of prey. At the same instant sunlight glances off the flanks of the pyramid, a dazzling beam that washes everything away in a blaze of burning white.

When your vision clears the horizon is empty. The pyramid is no longer near at hand. Turning, you spot a tiny wedge of scintillant light in the far distance. Was it you who moved, transported across miles in an instant by some metagrobolizing sorcery? Or did the pyramid itself apport away?

#### 1231

You run up the avenues of steps that lead to the plaza. Trees lie broken across the path, splintered by the unceasing barrage of catapult stones with which the raiders bombarded the city before sending in their war bands.

You can hear shouting from around the hillside - really just a single continuous roar of rage and glee and pain and terror all stirred into one horrible maelstrom.

The hand-to-hand fighting hasn't reached this far inland yet, but you see your share of dead bodies. Some must have been thrown from their homes when the catapult barrage started. They lie limbless or headless, white with mortar dust and red with blood. Other bodies recline on the streets as if they had only fainted. Getting closer, you notice the marks of slingshot that has drilled into their skulls or pierced their hearts with small, neat, bloodless puncture wounds. Some of the slingshot was stamped with messages - 'pig' or 'vermin' or 'insect' - as a last sneering curse from killer to victim.

The plaza is packed with refugees. The injured are being carried to the temple steps. Among the crowd you notice a few of the city guard. Caught unawares by the ferocity of the raiders' attack, they seem as cowed as the rest.

#### 1236

The rest of the day passes without incident, and as you make camp Stapes starts to tell one of his fireside tales. 'Ever hear of the battle standard of the Myrmidons? It was given to them by Achilles himself outside the walls of Troy, some say, and they carried it at their head of their legions on a hundred campaigns of conquest. What a prize! It was the very honour and glory of the Myrmidon army embodied in a staff that they never allowed to fall into enemy hands in all the centuries their city stood.'

'What happened to it?'

'Nothing lasts forever, does it? No, you have that, I'm not particularly hungry. Anyway, the city fell to invaders and a small band retreated into the desert with the standard. Bad luck for them that they strayed into the patch of desert where the Spartoi live. Well, not live, exactly. Myrmidons were the hardest bastards you could hope to face - as you'd expect of Achilles's own companions - but the Spartoi outnumbered them ten to one. They were skinned and so in a way they carried the standard to triumph in the Spartoi homeland. At least, their pelts did.'

Seeing your horrified look in the dancing light of the campfire, he nods. 'They wear their enemies' skins, the Spartoi. That's how you can tell the chieftains of the phylai, as they're the ones clad in fresh skin.'

The next day he is up before you. As he hefts his pack you notice it must be quite a weight for such a skinny, loose-limbed fellow.

#### 1251

You single out some of the ringleaders among the women persecuting the pregnant girl. 'You fools,' you declaim loudly. 'You risk bringing the wrath of the gods down on all our heads.'

'What are you talking about?' snaps back a tall raw-boned woman carrying two infants.

You look around uneasily. 'Not here. Come outside.'

Intrigued, they follow you onto the temple steps where you weave a story about having seen a beam of light shine through the girl's window one night. 'I saw Zeus in the form of a moth fly into her bedroom,' you say in hushed tones.

'Nonsense!' says the tall woman.

'Don't be so quick, Thosa,' says another. 'It's well known the Lord of the Sky has a fondness for mortal girls.'

'But you can't honestly - '

'Zeus is known as the upholder of oaths,' you say hastily. 'Who would dare doubt any oath that mentions him?'

You are careful to make no such oath, but they don't notice that. Credulity appears in their eyes. You can see that some, like the formidable Thosa, don't believe a word of it - but they balk at saying anything. Hard times have made the mob dangerously devout, and anyone who expresses scepticism might be ostracized or worse.

Later, while you are praying, the senior priest comes to bless you in person, having heard of your intervention on the girl's behalf.

Increase your blessings to three if you didn't have the maximum allowance already. Each blessing can be used to roll again when you fail an attribute check (Charm, Grace, Ingenuity or Strength) but only once in any given situation - so if you fail the reroll, you can't spend another blessing to try again.

#### 1265

The Amazons are lauded as a warrior-race of great courage and skill at arms, but known also for their ruthlessness to those they vanquish. #### 1271

After many hours a shadow passes swiftly overhead. You look up into a sky filled with stars. Did you stay here so long? As you get to your feet there is a sense of dislocation, as though the you of an hour ago had been taken apart and rebuilt - almost, but not quite, exactly as you were before.

#### 1283

'The image on the seal - '

'Don't tell me. An ant?'

Pausanias laughs. 'Not this time. It was the ancestral sigil of King Myrmidon's family. That same symbol was stamped on every official decree, vesting it with the divinely granted authority of Iskandria's ruling councils. The penalty for forging it was to die thrice.'

'How did that work?'

'Best not to ask. Let's just say that, short of arousing the wrath of the Furies, no crime was more abominated. The seal was the visible symbol of Iskandria's sovereignty.'

He shakes his head. 'It was chiselled from the walls when Iskandria fell, carried off by a contingent of city guards to a place of safety in the far west of Notus. But where they hid it is a secret now lost.'

'He is a son of Cerberus, the hound of Hades, but before he was even named he fell into the River Styx and drowned. Cerberus begged his master for aid and Hades returned the cub to life - of a sort, for he is what some call undead, a shadow of what he was in life, hence the name, but it is the shadow that is the stronger. His dead heart is filled with bloodlust, greed and rage. They say that those who hear his howl are doomed. And rightly so, for having drowned in the river Styx, Wolfshadow is invulnerable, though some say there is yet a way to slay him.'

#### 1286

High overhead - those circling specks are vultures. They expect you to meet your end up here. They could just be waiting in case you fall, but there are other signs that make you suspect a predator lurks nearby. The way that small mammals and lizards remain hidden even from your practiced hunter's eye. The paths scraped out between the rocks, suggesting something large and heavy often passes along these ridges. And the many caves in the hillside, all of their entrances swept clear of debris.

So you are not taken by surprise when a giant serpentine neck uncoils from the nearest cave. Venom-slavering jaws snap where you were standing an instant earlier. You are already jumping to another rock as a second head and then a third erupt from other caves. As the creature heaves its bulk out into the open you see it is not a nest of giant snakes but a single hydra.

You let it think you're running away, leading it down a ravine till it brushes past the pile of boulders you noticed on your way up, dislodging a small landslide that leaves the two leading heads crushed and pinned. Hissing, howling, thrashing madly in its efforts to free itself, the monster fails to notice you running towards the thickening in its torso where the necks all meet. A sharp splinter of stone driven into the monster's heart does the rest. You step back to avoid the flow of foul green blood that washes from the wound around your feet.

With a final shudder that shakes the mountainside, the hydra stiffens and goes still.

goto [**1694**](#HeadingNum1694) and tick the box before reading on.

#### 1287

You come face to face with a hideous white countenance hung about with writhing serpentine hair. But you hardly notice the snakes, nor the terrible demonic rictus, because you are transfixed by the eyes, gleaming dully like dying stars in the furthest reaches of space. The sight of them chills you, a coldness that spreads from your core to your extremities, and you feel your body stiffening into unyielding stone forever. You will never forget the gaze of Medusa.

#### 1288

You gather up the clothes and bury them in the sand a little way off. Then you settle down to wait.

After a while the crocodiles drag themselves up on shore and are astonished to find the clothes are gone.

'Somebody's pinched our gear!' cries one in very human outrage.

'That'd be me,' you say, stepping out of the bank of reeds.

The crocodiles - if that's what they are - glare at you. Their leader raises himself into a hunched bipedal posture and says, 'You nabbed our breeks, eh? The cheek of it. Eh, lads?'

Another nods, which is a strange thing to see a crocodile do, and says, 'We come down and do a bit of fishing, splash about a bit, maybe chomp a few passers-by. You'd think you could have some fun in your own backyard without some thieving newbug coming by and pinching your clothes.'

'You can't exactly complain if we eat you,' says a third in reasonable tone.

'Wouldn't you then be stuck in croc form?' you say. 'You see, I think you need those clothes. So promise to make a fair exchange, with no fangs or biting or blood involved, and I think we can all go away happier.'

They grumble, but finally agree to your terms.

#### 1313

As you ready the accoutrements of the ceremony, you are aware of the pressure of long generations that came before you. If Loutro had not imparted the secrets of the cult to you, all that would have been lost forever.

Your words echo up the cliff, calling to a goddess to whom none has made offerings in living memory. As you sing the final phrases, the echo dies away and there is silence.

Far off: a mutter that resounds from deep in the sky. Black clouds pile up against the sun. A wave of sudden, startling cool washes around you - a breeze that brings with it a fresh, wet odour alien to these barren rocks.

A cold sting on your cheek. You wipe it away. A drop of water. And another.

And then, erupting over the lip of the precipice a thousand metres above, a spume of white water that is followed by a raging torrent. You hurry back out of the way as the waterfall plunges to fill the basin at the foot of the cliffs and spills over to gush away southwards.

#### 1325

'How precarious is mortal existence,' says a voice out of the darkness beside you. 'A toehold on a little ledge, and below you the abyss.'

'Who's there?' With your throat tightened by fear, it comes out as a squeak.

'One who is always at your side. Your constant companion.'

Your foot slips. You press yourself closer to the wall, shuddering, as you steady yourself more carefully. But your foot slips again, and now realization comes like vertigo. The ledge is getting narrower, shrinking from a few centimetres to just one centimetre. Less.

'Let me by,' you croak.

'A metaphor,' she replies with infinite slowness. 'Mortals have an unreliable purchase on life, and when it is threatened what impedes them from saving themselves? Guilt.'

You slip again and this time there's no recovering. As you fall back into the void, the last thing you hear is the beating of wings.

#### 1329

#### 1335

'Why horn?' you wonder. 'It's a bit hard to lie your head on, isn't it?'

'You attach a pillow to it,' she explains in a tone that Muses use to mortals and mothers use to very small children. 'And it's horn because that supposedly signifies true dreams, whereas ivory would bring false dreams.'

'I have a question.'

She nods. 'It's a belief based on a similarity of phonetics between different word pairs.'

'A pun, you mean.'

'Precisely. The old word for ivory sounded like deceit, and the word for horn suggested something coming true.'

'Interesting. That wasn't my question, though.'

She raises an eyebrow quizzically.

'What I wanted to ask,' you go on, 'is what's the difference between ivory and horn?'

You look around. There is no living thing to be seen. A soft sough of wind deepens the sense of eerie isolation. Perhaps it would have been wiser never to have come back here.

#### 1338

'Impostor!'

The shout whipcracks across the amphitheatre and all heads turn. From the ranks of nobles beside the King, one has risen to denounce you. It is Malleus, the former captain of sentries who now wears the skin he cut off your dying body.

The crowd looks from one of you to the other. Seen together, it's obvious which is the real Malleus. The skin he wears is dry and leathery from the curing process. Stitching stands out along his torso and limbs. His lips sag horribly around the grinning mouth of the skull underneath. His eyes are just glass beads.

'Then who,' thunders the King, 'is this?'

Malleus strides to the balustrade overlooking the arena floor. 'A Myrmidon, Your Majesty, come to steal the standard we wrested from them in ancient times.'

Good guess. The King seizes one of the battle standards from the rack behind him, one whose gilded finial is shaped like the head and mandibles of an ant.

'Let this be the last thing you behold, then,' he cries wrathfully. 'Here it is, just beyond your reach. You'll see it as your blood pumps out onto these sands. Malleus - slay this interloper who wears your face.'

Malleus draws his sword and swings nimbly down to the arena floor. What's your plan?

Fight goto [**1199**](#HeadingNum1199)

Flee goto [**1066**](#HeadingNum1066)

#### 1339

#### 1340

The crowd shuns one young woman who approaches the altar with bowed head, kneels and utters a hesitant prayer. The older women look daggers at her and one makes an imprecatory gesture in the girl's face. As the girl gets to her feet, you see that she is pregnant.

'That baby will be a raider's bastard,' says a woman as the girl goes meekly by.

You look at her, shocked. 'She was raped?'

'Hah. She might say that's the way of it, but a lot of those young chits got very pally with the raiders, if you take my meaning.'

The older women are following the girl. Some of them look almost insane with hatred. You sense there could be trouble.

#### 1341

Fortunately there is a pillar close to the spot where the sand caved in, so the climb down to the cavern floor is not too difficult. You wince as the brittle bones of the dead soldiers crunch under your feet. A bronze helm, green and eaten by corrosion, rattles across the smooth flagstones of the floor.

Lighting a torch, you can now see that the chamber stretches for hundreds of metres. The roof is a mesh of iron filings held in place by powerful magnetic attraction, suspended between metal pillars. It dawns on you that those pillars extend above ground and are disguised by a covering of stonework, which accounts for why the tors around here seemed too regularly spaced to be natural rock formations.

You turn to face the nearest wall and your surmise about the dead soldiers is proved correct. They must have been Myrmidons, because on the wall is a large disk that you recognize from illustrations as the seal of Iskandria. Once it was mounted above the north gate of the city, so the legends say, but it was lost when barbarian hordes overwhelmed Iskandria's defences. These soldiers must have carried it here for safekeeping - a final act of dutiful obedience carried out with their last breaths.

You step closer. The seal is wider than a hoplite's shield and as thick as the span of your hand. Even so, you think you ought to be able to carry it if you can get it on your back. But when you take hold of it and try to pull it from the wall, you find that it's stuck fast by the same magnetic pull that supports the chamber's roof. Straining with all your might, you manage to move it a hair's breadth before it slips from your fingers and clamps back onto the metal wall.

'Look what's dropped in,' booms a voice behind you.

You turn to behold the strangest sight you've come across in all of this strange realm. Surrounding you as you press back against the wall are four looming columnar shapes, each nearly reaching to the chamber roof. And your legs almost give way as you realize in shock that they are four enormous fingers.

goto [**435**](#HeadingNum435)

#### 1344

The kitchen is almost a court in its own right, less majestic than the throne room and without the grand furnishings, but with just as precise a hierarchy.

At the central table, where fifty could sit down for dinner without touching shoulders, the cook Mageira sees all and issues a constant stream of orders to her army of assistants and scullions.

Beside the wide hearth, in which you could roast three oxen end to end, sits the old nanny, Baubo, whose status is equal to Mageira's. She came to the palace with Queen Metanira, whom she raised from infancy.

At the other end of the pecking order are such lowly servants as the maid, Piretria, who is currently churning butter, and the baby's wet-nurse, Kybele, who is taking advantage of a brief moment of leisure to pour herself a cup of barley water.

As it's a hot day, the door to the garden stands open and a moment ago you noticed the gardener bringing in a basket of herbs.

#### 1352

You have passed beyond agony into a state of raw tortured nerve-endings that could be bliss or nightmare, either the suspension of feeling before plunging into a abyss of pain or else the comforting numbness that precedes oblivion.

A shadow across the sun. The beating of powerful wings. So the vultures have come...

No, not vultures. Soft footsteps walk towards where you lie in the blood-soaked sand. You cannot move.

'I had not hoped to regard you in this bony light,' says a familiar voice.

Your skull is caressed, then gently tugged free of what sinew remains on the bones. You look into the shadowed face of Nemesis and see the flicker of her melancholy smile. 'I knew this one. Quite a saucy thief. Do you want to turn those light fingers to a better cause?'

You can't speak, but you dimly wonder what Nemesis means.

'Steal something the Spartoi stole from the followers of swift-footed Achilles,' she goes on. 'It would be hard if you wore a coat of flesh, but with just your bare bones on show you could walk in among the Spartoi unnoticed.'

She's offering you the choice to be brought back as a living skeleton.

#### 1371

#### 1383

As you step into the tavern you experience a strange dream-like sense of dislocation. Standing by the bar in the shade at the back of the room you see yourself.

No, not yourself. There are stitches in the skin, a yellowish tinge that suggests it has been left to cure in the sun. The face hangs slack; the eyes are balls of glass that gleam without expression. The fingers are loose like an overlarge glove.

'Drinks on you now you're a lord?' says the skeletal barkeep.

'Go on, then,' says Stapes, glancing round to calculate how many he's buying for. 'Just one round.'

Several Spartoi amble over to the bar to collect their tankards. 'It's a fine piece of flesh,' says one appreciatively.

'You need a steady hand for the skinning,' says Stapes, 'otherwise it's all a waste of effort.'

They crowd round and he basks in their compliments. He obviously enjoys lording it over lesser Spartoi who don't have skins to wear. You hear him telling the story of how he killed you and took your skin. In his version he's a lot braver than in reality. Drugging you and murdering you in your sleep is not mentioned.

One old Spore with chipped ribs ventures a criticism. 'I don't know about the nose. Bit too big.'

'Nonsense,' you blurt out. 'It's a noble nose. It shows strength of character.'

They all look round, Stapes included, and you feel a moment of dread until you realize that he can't possibly recognize you.

'Quite right!' says Stapes, clapping a hand on your clavicle and offering you a tankard. 'Come on, drink up.'

You spend a few minutes talking to your own dead face until you are able to extricate yourself and slip out of the tavern. goto [**126**](#HeadingNum126)

'You pack of sour harridans,' you tell them. 'Don't pretend to be pious and morally outraged when the simple truth is that you're jealous of this woman's youth. The raiders didn't give you a second look, huh? They'd rather carry off a pig or a sack of grain than one of you. Too bad. Don't take out your own shortcomings on this innocent girl.'

The ring of staring faces, previously dark with anger, now go white with shock. For a moment you think your sermon has had an effect - and so it has, but not what you intended. The women's expressions contort into screaming hatred. Seizing the pregnant girl they haul her outside and, grabbing chunks of broken masonry from the debris left by the raiders, they chant horrible curses while stoning her to death.

An elderly woman who in normal times might be a pleasant grandmotherly type storms up to you and laughs horribly. 'She can have her baby in Hades now!'

Sick with horror at this atrocity committed in broad daylight, you hurry away without a backward glance at the bloody corpse in the middle of the plaza.

goto [**1007**](#HeadingNum1007)

#### 1417

#### 1418

The thundering of bony hooves, the rattling of chariot wheels, the crack of chain whips, the harsh curses and shouted boasts, the clang as the contestants swerve against each other - all evoke a curiously hollow and funereal reflection of a chariot race among the living. Yet it seems to please the onlookers, who caper in excitement and give shrill cries of encouragement to their favourite.

Roll two dice to determine who wins. On a score of 2 to 7 it's Ilium. On a roll of 8 or more it's Talus.

If you have the title **Champion of the Amazons** then you are an expert judge of each contestant's form and can nudge the dice roll by 1 in either direction.

If the charioteer you backed lost the race, you forfeit the amount you bet. If you picked the winner, you get back your initial stake plus 70% (if you backed Ilium) or plus 130% (if you backed Talus).

You adjust fractions in the bookies' favour. For example, suppose you had put 12 pyr on Talus to win and he did. You would get back 12 pyr (your original bet) plus winnings of 15 pyr (130% of 12, rounded down).

Stay for the next race goto [**1474**](#HeadingNum1474)

#### 1421

'In the ancient world, one name was synonymous with glory - the name of the First Ancestor, who united the whole realm of Notus into a single empire. And yet now that name is lost. Not even the sphinxes know it, who hoard secrets along with gold. So glory turns to dust. When Odysseus encountered Achilles's ghost in the land of the dead, this is what the hero told him:

_"Rather I'd choose laboriously to bear_

_A weight of woes, and breathe the vital air,_

_A slave to some poor serf that toils for bread,_

_Than reign as sceptred monarch of the dead."_

'If even Achilles himself spurned glory, he who earned more of it than any man at arms, what value can it really have?'

#### 1433

'Not my job,' he grunts. 'You turn the tap on that brings the river back, so the port will get back on its feet, and maybe the merchant ships will come. And then, and only then, it might be worth climbing all those stairs to light a blooming great lamp.'

#### 1437

They're a sour-looking crowd, but the moment you sing the first lines of the lament their faces go rapt with attention. After a few verses they're gawping like little kids on the first day of school. By the time you reach the closing refrain they're sobbing and hugging each other.

'I like how it experiments with a divine point of view,' announces one huge bruiser, wiping his eyes.

'Giving us the voice of the tutelary goddess as she bewails her lost city,' says another. 'I loved the line about "my city, like a tent, like a garden shed, torn up and my things scattered". You don't have to be a deity to know what that feels like, eh?'

Another scary-looking brute nods vigorously. 'Very true. It's a masterful device for instilling an ironic distance from the audience, who are of course mortal.'

The whistle blows for the afternoon shift and as one they get to their feet and slouch back to the mines. Except for one snaggle-toothed roustabout who sidles up to you shyly.

#### 1438

'Lord Malleus,' calls a cobbler as you walk past his shop, 'I wasn't expecting you so early.'

Malleus - the Spartoi patrol leader who killed and skinned you in your last incarnation. So he is due here soon. You should make yourself scarce.

'Please, my lord,' insists the cobbler, taking you by the arm, 'The boots you ordered are ready. If you'd like to try them on...'

You suffer yourself to be sat down while he fiddles about fitting the boots and adjusting the straps. You glance up - across the marketplace you see yourself, or at least a fleshless and sagging version of your face stretched across an unfamiliar skull. Malleus - !

'I just remembered something,' you say, jumping up.

'But, my lord,' protests the cobbler. 'I'll have the straps adjusted in just a minute.'

'Fine. I'll only be a minute. Is there a back way?'

Puzzled, he points to a flap in the rear of the shop. You pause as you duck through. 'I'll be right back. And don't bore me with any twaddle about "oh, you're back" or "I said it would only take a minute" or I'll take my custom elsewhere. Got it?'

You leave him with his jaw hanging open and hurry away from the marketplace. It wouldn't do to bump into Malleus and blow your cover.

goto [**126**](#HeadingNum126)

#### 1439

He catches your eye and then nods towards the sea. 'Go on in, the water's lovely.'

#### 1440

At last your turn comes. There is no run-up but you are given two small dumbbell-shaped weights, called halteres, which you can swing to give extra momentum.

Make a Grace roll at a difficulty of 9. That is, roll two six-sided dice and add your Grace, and you need to score 9 or more. (For this contest you cannot use the bonus of any items you possess, just your raw Grace attribute.)

'Nothing's really black and white, is it?'

#### 1443

It's no use. They've got the wrong end of the stick. As you leave the chamber they are still deliberating the increased naval budget and what design of warships to use.

#### 1445

You throw up your hands. 'Enough. You win.'

Xanthe nods, smiling broadly, then without warning delivers a punch that sends stars exploding and whirling across your vision. With her foot she shoves you over the side of the platform and you fall limp into the arms of the crowd. Barely conscious, you have no strength to resist as they haul you over the back of a horse. You are carried far from the camp and flung down in the dust.

As they ride off, a voice calls back, 'We're merciful this once, but don't ever return if you want to live.'

#### 1446

In these dead hills, where the wind shrieking in the hollow caves is the only voice and the cascade of falling pebbles the only movement, a traveller might easily leave a piece of their soul pinned up against the uncaring roof of heaven.

You survey the skeleton of the dragon you slew. Its bones are becoming one with the rocks. It is time you returned to the world of men or you too might fade off into myth.

#### 1449

'Something smells good,' you say to her.

'That bread, eh? The aroma just makes your mouth water. And it rises so easily these days. Cakes as well. Look at these jam tarts - oh, just one, then. It's getting so that the food cooks itself and I'll be out of a job.'

'Is it the season? New farming methods?'

She laughs. 'The last new farming methods came in when this palace was a mud-brick hut. We don't hold with new-fangled ways in these parts.'

'What, then?'

'I think it's the weather. Since the night of the big storm the grain is so lush, the milk so rich, the meat so succulent - you'd think this was the kitchen on Mount Olympus.'

#### 1450

'Wait! Wait!'

'All life is a stream, my friend. We are leaves swept along in her current.'

He tells you that he has been taking the worship of Tethys to other regions of Vulcan's realm. 'Her realm, really, of course, for without water to quench the makings of his furnace, the god of smiths would have nothing to offer the world.'

'You seem to be thriving, Loutro.'

'Yes, I am.'

'Then forbear from criticism of Vulcan, is my advice. At least in this strange universe of webs and nets and chains, he is master even of the Olympians.'

'For now, but I have heard - ' Loutro looks around. 'But this is not the place. We could be overheard. Stay well.'

He hurries off, glancing furtively around him.

#### 1453

You approach the dark opening that was the lair of Venomtail. The bronze door has been slid to one side, revealing a square chamber hewn out of the earth in aeons past by unknown hands, and the den of a terrible monster now thankfully slain. The smell is acrid and foul, like Greek fire in a cess pit.

Several skeletal remains line one side of the chamber but also a body that seems to be still alive... As you draw near, you can see it twitching, presumably as Venomtail's poison begins to wear off. Perhaps you disturbed the giant scorpion's dinner. Suddenly, the figure jumps up and runs for the doorway, screaming wildly. As she reaches the door, she sees the slain corpse of Venomtail, pulls up short, and turns to stare at you in amazement, jaw agape. The she turns tail and flees.

'Wait, let me help you,' you shout, but she is too crazed with fear to take notice and has soon disappeared into the sands of the desert. You turn your attention to the scorpion's lair. There is nothing else of interest, save for a hatch of copper set into the ground, with the same picture of an eye radiating fireballs as is to be found above the doorway, carved into it. Upon it these words are also etched:

_My first is the greatest warrior_

_My second is a judge of the Dead_

_My third is vengeful Fury_

_My fourth are the children of Arachne_

_And warlike is my whole_

_Who am I?_

Take the first letter of your answer, give it its corresponding number in the alphabet, add them all together and turn to that paragraph. If it is correct, it will say so, if not, return here and choose again. If you cannot solve the riddle, you will have to come back here another day.

#### 1454

'Thermyra's just a supply base. We brought the whole fleet through from the Great Sinkhole. And back again.'

'Eh? How's that work, then?'

'You ask too many questions. Let's just say that if you were to find a certain coral basin and you stirred it counter-clockwise at the stroke of midnight, you'd see something to tell your kids about.'

'If I knew where my kids were!'

As you steal away they both chuckle quietly in the bosom of night.

#### 1459

'Jason, of the Argonauts fame,' begins Stapes, 'did just about as many famous deeds as Herakles, quite a few of them before breakfast. Case in point, King Aeëtes had agreed to give Jason the golden fleece but then started to have second thoughts. "Do something heroic for it," he said. "All right," said Jason. So the King told him to yoke two fire-breathing automaton bulls, plough the field of Ares, and then sow it with dragon's teeth - all before sundown. Now, heroes can do that sort of thing, but when he sowed the teeth Jason got a crop he didn't bargain on - a phalanx of skeletal Spartoi warriors. He was dead tired after the day's labours, and a score of Spartoi are enough to give any hero qualms, but he distracted them with an egg and - '

'With what?'

'An egg. Dragon's egg. Important to Spartoi. Circle of life sort of thing.'

'So he got the fleece. Then what?'

Stapes shakes his head. 'I should've picked a shorter story. We'll be up all night with the voyage of the Argonauts. Tell you what, I'll pick it up tomorrow evening.'

'Haven't you told that same story before?' goto [**360**](#HeadingNum360)

#### 1460

The bird suddenly spreads its wings - a span of at least three metres, a breathtaking banner of crimson against the pale blue sky - and takes to the air. It climbs rapidly before swooping in a long graceful curve that carries it out of sight around the peak. At no point did it utter a sound.

When you go closer you see drops of amber-coloured resin on the bush where it stripped away the bark. There's a sweet odour like pine. Other marks on the bush indicate that the bird must come here from time to time to collect resin.

There's nothing else of interest this high up, so you begin to make your way back down the mountainside.

goto [**199**](#HeadingNum199)

#### 1461

You leave the pot wedged among some rocks. What to bait it with? Hands obviously cannot eat, but you figure that they are endlessly inquisitive so you leave a pile of polished pebbles in the trap.

You deliberately make camp some distance away. In the evening, as you eat a meagre supper, there's a creaking and rustling, followed by a frenzied thrashing.

You run to the trap. Too late. The hand has already broken free and is scuttling away into the darkness like a big pallid spider. But it has left one of its long nails broken off in the wreckage.

Delete the **lobster pot** and replace it with a **broken trap**. You might be able to get it mended somewhere.

The nail is more interesting. You might have expected it to be dirty and ragged, but it is long, graceful and manicured like a lyre-player's. Add the **Orphean nail** to your possessions.

goto [**1451**](#HeadingNum1451)

#### 1462
