'Better run it for a few minutes to flush out all the muck,' you decide.

The work has been a challenging mental exercise. If your Ingenuity score is +1 or less, increase it by 1. Then goto [**1007**](#HeadingNum1007)

#### 1463

You approach the pregnant girl, greet her, and put your arm around her protectively. 'I am the father,' you tell the crowd. 'Who is calling me a raider?' You glare at them defiantly.

A large matronly woman speaks up. 'Not calling you a raider,' she says sullenly. 'But I know what I seen, and - '

You go up and press your face close to hers. 'And that's our misfortune, isn't it? If you saw it and you did nothing, then you can keep your fat mouth shut now. And as for the rest of you, find something better to do than spread malice. It's only the mercy of the gods that my wife didn't lose our baby when that raider assaulted her. If anyone goes around saying that the baby is a raider's bastard, know now that I'll call the curse of the deity on your head.'

The theatrical gesture you make towards the idol above the altar is what seals it. None of them wants to risk divine fury after everything they've suffered already. Muttering, they slink away like a pack of cowed hounds.

Later, while you are praying, the senior priest comes to bless you in person, having heard of your intervention on the girl's behalf.

Increase your blessings to three if you didn't have the maximum allowance already. Each blessing can be used to roll again when you fail an attribute check (Charm, Grace, Ingenuity or Strength) but only once in any given situation - so if you fail the reroll, you can't spend another blessing to try again.

#### 1476

He nods thoughtfully. 'Oceanos once submerged this whole region, but he was made to draw back to the world's edges. The deepest sea bed is a desert just like this - but lightless, for Helios has no sway there against the much greater power of Nyx, Goddess of Night.'

#### 1481

You make swift progress along the trail of moonlight. Ahead stretches the great lake. The path of light continues right out from the shore, a shining streak of silver under a sky of velvet dark. You walk out along it for what seems like hours, your feet skimming the black mirror of the still waters. The occasional muffled splash of something unseen far out in the darkness only deepens the sense of hushed unreality.

There is something ahead: a sculpture in the middle of the lake. It is made of entwined marble serpents, curving and looping and knotting around each other in a complex pattern. From the serpents' mouths spurt fountains of moon-washed spray, and their tails make a set of marble steps the colour of milk that rise towards the summit of the structure. #### 1489

A rhino can move much faster on land than any human being. Knowing that, you make for the nearest tree and scramble up, getting a firm hold just in time to stop yourself being hurled out of the branches by the splintering impact of the rhino's charge.

Again and again it batters the trunk, with such savage fury and strength that you're sure it must be about to tear the tree up by its roots. Chips of wood go flying and you can smell the sap in the warm air. Better that than blood, anyway. After a few minutes the rhino suddenly breaks off its attack, glances back towards the herd, and jogs off as if nothing had happened.

You wait until the herd has moved off a little way before climbing down and continuing on to towards the lake.

You pause in the shade of a small orchard beside the wall of a cottage. At the kitchen door, a matronly woman is throwing orange peel from her apron to a drove of hungry piglets.

'Fancy a brew?' she says, beckoning you to a chair on the porch.

You are given a goblet of kykeon, a peasant drink made of goat milk, barley, herbs and honey. It has a refreshing minty taste.

'That's the pennyroyal. An old family recipe,' says the woman, who tells you she is Ma Krithare. She keeps a sort of inn along with her husband, an amiable man of few words whom everybody just calls Pops.

If you are wounded then resting here for a day or two and enjoying Ma's cooking is enough to fully restore you. You can erase a tick in the Wound box on your Adventure Sheet.

#### 1498

'Hark at shorty,' laughs Middle. 'But you're right. Can't take anything away from a brave mortal. They're so easily squashed.'

'I'll try not to think about that next time I'm in danger,' you say.

#### 1512

#### 1514

Passing down a gulch choked with soft white sand, you hear a clattering noise among the rocks that jut up on either side. It sounds like somebody slowly dragging a sack full of loose sticks.

Climbing up to investigate, you find a half-built deadfall trap comprising a small boulder with some sticks that must be intended to prop it up so that it can be rolled down on anyone in the ravine below. Protruding from under the boulder are two skeletal lower legs. Marks in the dust tell you that something has been dragged away from here, and looking around the rocks you soon catch up with an animated skeleton with its lower legs snapped off. When it heard you coming it tried to hide, but now it starts desperately crawling away.

It's an easy matter to catch up to the skeleton. Even seeing your feet planted on either side, it keeps on clawing at the ground. It's determined, you have to give it that.

'I suppose you meant that trap for me,' you say. 'Only you ended up hobbling yourself instead.'

#### 1526

As soon as the goat has finished its meal it looks around for water and, finding none, begins to utter plaintive bleats that sound for all the world like somebody strangling the bagpipes.

For a long time nothing happens, until you begin to feel like climbing down and killing the wretched goat yourself. Abruptly it falls silent. Peeking out from cover, you see a huge sinuous creature hopping and fluttering over the sand. The goat lies still, slain by the cockatrice's stare. Snapping its beak, glaring all around but happily not thinking to look up, the creature creeps closer to its prey at the base of the rock directly below you.

#### 1556

The market square is packed with hundreds of ragged, hollow-cheeked survivors of the raid. They shuffle despairingly from stall to stall, pleading with the shopkeepers for food.

'I have no bread,' you hear a baker saying as he tries to pull down his shutters.

'Don't give us that,' snarls an emaciated old woman. 'We know you've got some round the back.'

'He's hoarding cakes!' shouts a man with his arm in a sling. 'He'll only sell if you can pay in gold.'

'Where are the younger folk?' you ask the baker as the disappointed crowd disperse. 'Most of these people are elderly. And I haven't seen any children.'

'Many young men were killed in the fighting,' he says. 'The young women? Worse - the raiders carried them off. As for children, didn't you see the new graves on the hillside back there? Those scum didn't just want to plunder our city, they wanted to wipe us out.'

There is a food bank at the side of the market, set up by the syndics of the town to provide for those who are too sick, infirm or injured to work. The tiny rations that can be spared for each person are barely enough: a palmful of bread, a small cup of thin soup, and a few scraps of smoked fish.

#### 1557

It's as good a way as any to choose a route. The coin comes up heads and you take the arch indicated. You step through into a shaft with a spiral ramp corkscrewing towards the daylight.

Up and up you climb. It seems endless. The opening at the top of the shaft gets no nearer, the light no brighter, and now you are dizzy with the turning and turning and turning of the ramp.

You reel, reaching out for the wall with a stab of panic in case you might topple off into the shaft. But there is no wall. No shaft either. You are standing in the middle of a featureless red desert with a line of tors snaggled along the horizon.

You take a step forward and stumble against something. A chest, buried in the sand. Hauling it up, you open it to discover a pair of rust-spotted iron bracers. If cleaned up they would be magnificent - but worthless without the rest of the suit of armour.

#### 1560

How long has it been? You can think of nothing but the search. Each plan comes to you like divine inspiration, and you're sure that this time you have the perfect method for locating the Amazon caravan - and each time it ends in failure.

One day a group of children come to gawp at you as you make calculations in the sand with a stick. 'Perhaps you want me to teach you the secrets of the wild?' you say to them. 'See, these arrows are the direction of the seasonal winds, and these show subterranean springs, while this line here - '

'Nah,' says the oldest. 'We just wanted to see the fool everyone talks about.'

Your confidence has been dealt an irrecoverable blow. Now you see yourself as these barefoot children do - no genius, merely an addled obsessive. Lose 1 from your Ingenuity score.

#### 1561

'A strange business indeed. There was a terrible roaring of wind that went on all night. It ripped the roofs clean off some outbuildings and who knows where they ended up. I thought the crops would be flattened, but when we looked out the next morning they were straight and tall and even riper-looking than they had been the day before.'

'Very heavy rain?'

'No rain at all. And only a single lightning-strike. I saw it myself. It crashed down from the peak and skidded right across the fields. Ball lightning, they call it.'

'That must have scorched the crops.'

He shakes his head. 'Unscathed, like I said. And here's the strange bit - there was no peal of thunder, and where the lightning had flashed across the field, next day there was a trail of white poppies.'

#### 1602

Psyche has a reputation as one of the most incisive and erudite thinkers of her age, so perhaps it's with some surprise that you find she is also one of the most beautiful.

She is eager to hear about your travels, listening raptly and interjecting from time to time to explain something you've seen or to suggest other places you should visit. Yet there's a cloud over her. When she pauses to reflect, her gaze seems to turn inward and her expression becomes troubled.

'I am apart from my true love,' she admits when pressed. 'Without him I can take no joy in food or drink or learning. Even this parchment-scented air, the music of softly rustling pages, and the atmosphere of silent contemplation bring me only the shadow of pleasure.'

'Does he live far from here?'

She nods. 'Very far. And, too, his mother disapproves.' She brightens as a thought occurs to her. 'I can't spare time to wander in search of him, but your life carries you far and wide. Will you be my go-between and take him something?'

You dart down the tunnel. Along one wall of a vast chamber are alcoves piled with gold and jewels. You seize a handful and run back outside in time to greet the Sphinx as it rounds the base of the pyramid.

'The crafty devil hid under the sand!' you shout. 'You can catch him, though - he's trapped himself inside.'

It gives a low growl like thunderheads massing for a storm and pushes its colossal body back down the tunnel. You add the 200 pyr you grabbed to your money and then hurry away.

#### 1624

#### 1625

You follow the two fishermen along the tunnel, which rises and opens up into a small chamber in the heart of the rock.

An ivory idol stands here. It is a young woman who for a moment you thought was real, so perfectly is she fashioned. The ivory limbs are as smooth as real flesh. The folds of her chiton hang exactly as they would if made of cloth. And her pose is not the stiff attitude of a temple idol. The sculptor has carved her in an posture with neck arched and hand held over her uptilted lips as if lowering food into her mouth. It's a frozen instant of movement, caught by artistry of real genius.

'Sometimes I dangle a crab-paste sarnie in her hand as an offering, like,' says the older fisherman.

'A sandwich?'

'You got any better ideas?' he says.

Well, have you?

#### 1631

You land with a painful jolt that wrenches your shoulder. Tick the Wound box on your sheet if you were not wounded already. You clutch at the rock with your good arm but can find no purchase to climb out.

The sun sinks behind the peaks. In the blue twilight, a figure appears at the top of the crevasse. #### 1636

A curious little book of musings by the philosopher Peneius includes this snippet:

'When we see a frightful face, we are apt to impute to that person's heart the same dreadful feelings that are evoked in us. As we feel fear, so we think them fearsome.

'Consider, though, the feelings of her whom fate has shaped to spread terror. Could there not be loneliness? Melancholy? A need for love?

'Does the gorgon trace her fingers across the fossil features of those struck lifeless by her gaze? Does she weep to think that here might have been a friend, a companion, a lover? As Midas came to rue the touch that placed him apart from all others, so must the gorgon regret that she can never look with longing on another, for even to look is to kill the thing she loves.

'Unless, that is, the one she meets is her true love. For love conquers all, we know, and therefore it follows love must conquer the petrifying stare. The gorgon will know her true love because he is the one who can look back into her eyes and say, "We are together now."'

But then the oldest mouse pointed out a flaw in the plan: "Which of us is going to tie the bell on the cat?"'

#### 1659

A blaze of star-filled darkness surrounds you, great cliffs and waterfalls of pinprick light stretching into a void deeper than infinity.

Below stretches the whole world of mortals, moon-laced and slumbrous, with night pressed heavy on it. You raise a hand of coruscating white fire. All of your limbs are shining suns, and on your belt of strung blue giants hangs a sword whose blade is nebulae.

You are the constellation of Orion the Hunter. You are part of the night sky. And even as you realize this you see your arch-enemy, the constellation of the Scorpion, hurtling towards you across the gulf of endless space. Its sting, its pincers, must cross a million miles to strike you - but each breath takes less than half an epoch, and banked eternity gives way in an avalanche of now.

You are locked in a struggle to the death. What are your weapons?

#### 1662

The valley where the Great River ran now forms a deep, dry scar across the whole land of Notus. Along most of its course the valley is wide, allowing the imagination to picture a titanic, surging river whose banks must have been kilometres apart. At the point where you now stand, though, the walls of the valley are close together. A strong bowman could put an arrow clean across it.

'These would have been rapids,' you say to Loutro.

He nods. 'The third cataract.'

The sand here has a greenish tinge and sifts slowly along the channel where the river once flowed. It is cold to the touch, and softer than the gritty sand all around, but in no other way like water.

'The goddess Tethys had many children,' says Loutro. 'They are the countless canals and brooks and streams of the old world. Too many names to learn, so we call this cataract after her first-born, Scamander.'

#### 1667

#### 1681

'Are you mad?' you shout, throwing the door open. 'Give me that baby!'

The shovel falls with a clang to the stone floor. She turns with a fierce look, and you wonder how you could ever have taken her for a teenage girl. Whatever illusion disguised her before, now in the licking firelight her true nature is revealed. She is the goddess Demeter, Giver of Laws, Bringer of Harvests, one of the oldest and most revered of the Olympians.

You are on your knees before her. Behind you, roused by the commotion, the King and Queen enter and they too recognize her divinity. Keleos abases himself, pressing his face to the sooty hearthstone. Metanira kneels and folds her hands meekly. In the doorway, servants whimper in awe. The goddess stands resplendent in her full glory, no longer in the body of a young girl but now a living thunderbolt made flesh.

#### 1682

The torch you are carrying dims and goes out. Pitch darkness closes around you like a fist. There is a sound of huge beating wings, a sense of something hurtling towards you at inescapable speed. You are seized and spun through icy air, your legs kicking frantically at nothingness.

Now you can see again, though you are momentarily dazzled by the harsh white radiance that comes from the rock on which you've been set. It seems to be a high crag. You lean out to get a sense of how high up you have been carried.

It's not a crag. A long curve of chalk-white rock juts out above and below you in a huge crescent, but nothing connects it to the ground so very far below. You are suspended high in the night sky. Many miles below you can make out a few winking lights, scattered campfires and hearths in an endless sea of darkness.

'From here we can even look down on Olympus,' says a voice behind you. 'Nothing is higher than justice.'

You look round at the black-clad figure with her wings folded around her.

'You're calling yourself goddess of justice now? I thought you were only retribution.'

'Not only. I am also goddess of the new moon, as my mother is the night.'

'And how can I breathe up here on the moon?'

She smiles. 'You can't.'

#### 1692

After a day of exceptional heat, you are glad to see the sun declining in the sky. A freshening breeze, though hot as the gust from an oven, at least promises the cool of evening.

You make camp beside a monotonous jumble of rocks still shimmering with the sun's heat. 'We'll be glad of the warmth from this rubble when the night turns really cold,' you tell Stapes.

'I was going to tell you the story of Persephone in Hades,' he says as you lay out your bedrolls. 'You know that one? Hades carried her off and she was smart enough to refuse the food and drink he plied her with, knowing that to accept his hospitality meant she'd given consent to stay. But just as she was leaving, the gardener of hell ran out and ratted on her. "She ate four pomegranate seeds!" he said. And that's why she has to spend four months of every year down you-know-where.'

'Four seeds? Hardly seems worth eating.'

Stapes reels back in exaggerated amazement. 'Pomegranate? Nectar of the gods. Better than nectar. You haven't lived till you've tasted my lamb and pomegranate stew. Tell you what, you tell tonight's story and I'll cook. I've got some dried lamb and some pomegranate seeds in my bag here.'

'Okay.' goto [**666**](#HeadingNum666)

'I have no stories.' goto [**1274**](#HeadingNum1274)

#### 1693

It is sweaty work digging a pit in the hard-packed earth, but you continue to chip away with pick and shovel until you have a pit deep enough to bury an elephant. After lining the bottom of the pit with sharp spikes of obsidian and flint, you rig up a deadfall that will crush anything that falls into the trap. The finishing touch is a piece of stretched canvas on which you scatter sand and pebbles. It's hard to tell it from the adjacent ground even when you're the one who dug it.

Staking out a goat, you sit back among a jumble of rocks to wait for the cockatrice. Towards sunset you hear the gravid slithering of its tail across the gritty sand. Peering out from your hiding place, you watch as it first kills the goat with its stare, then falls hungrily on the carcass. The canvas tears under its weight as it crosses the pit trap. It drops into the pit and is half hidden from view. Its screeches of pain rattle off the surrounding rocks.

Thrashing its wings, the wounded cockatrice starts to rise out of the pit and for a moment you think it might escape. But it dislodges the massive boulder you placed as a deadfall, and that rolls down to crush it. Even so, pinned in the pit between the boulder and the lacerating spikes, it is a long time dying. Boiling spittle flies from its beak. Its tail lashes out, scattering waves of sand and splintering boulders into rubble. Finally its struggles subside, it gives a final spasm, and lies still.

goto [**1531**](#HeadingNum1531)

#### 1695

You fashion splints from the sticks the maimed Spartoi was using to make his trap. Using those and some strips of leather from your backpack, you reattach his legs and bind them securely.

He sways to his feet. 'We Spartoi would never do such a thing,' he says, astonished. 'To show mercy to a foe who tried to kill us... It's unthinkable.'

'Well, you'll have to get it into your head. There should be plenty of room in there.'

He stares at you, then suddenly gives a peal of laughter. 'This is your flesh-and-blood humour! I like it. From now on, you can count on Hyperenor of the Triquetrum phyle for undying loyalty. And when it comes to undying loyalty, you can't beat a skeleton.'

It's a joke of sorts. He's learning.

#### 1696

There is no fire in the hearth, only ash. Outside is the cold blue of a winter's evening. The house feels as if something vital has gone out of it.

Years have passed. You are fully grown. Beside you, your mother is no longer the robust force of nature you remember but a thin little old lady.

'Two coins,' she says, holding them up. 'It's all we have to buy supper.'

It's only now you become aware of your brothers and sisters, all younger than you, huddled in silence at the back of the room.

'Give them to me, mother, and I'll fetch us something.'

'But then, how will he pay the ferryman?'

And it's with dread that you follow her eyes to the table, where the sheeted body of your father lies. Those two coins, placed on his eyes, would give his spirit the means to cross the river into the afterlife.

'Or should they go as an offering to the gods, who must look on us kindly if we are to survive?' wonders your mother. She sounds so lost and bewildered that, as you dream this, you must surely stir and sob in your slumber.

#### 1701

#### 1702

Get the codeword **_Offer_** and lose the codeword **_Ohone_** if you had it. If you were carrying **dragon's teeth** or a **dragon's egg** those are gone, but Stapes left your other possessions, having no use for them.

It takes some getting used to, holding body and soul together when your body is just a collection of articulated bones with neither skin nor tendon to knit them. At first you are reeling and staggering like a scarecrow in a high wind, but gradually you learn the jerky prowling lope of a living skeleton.

As for your soul - it is crusted and dry like clinker, a dead remnant of the fount of life that once flowed through you. Lose all your blessings, as the gods do not smile on the undead. In whatever is to come you are on your own.

You stumble through the desert, shuddering to hear your own limbs rattling like hollow sticks. A weathered hillside has crumbled away, exposing old sarcophagi from a lost civilization. By rights a body such as you now wear should lie still and silent in one of those cool graves, not go prancing horribly across the dunes.

If you investigate the sarcophagi, note **1490** as your Current Location and goto [**486**](#HeadingNum486)

#### 1704

The battle rages for quite a time, with you dodging tail strikes, and ducking under its pincers whilst trying to land a blow on either its vulnerable mouth parts, or its eyes, of which it has several. But in the end, all it takes is for you to take one small step out of place, and the tail of the scorpion drives down into your shoulder, shattering your collar bone. Even worse than that agonising pain, a burning venom is injected into your bloodstream. You can feel your body parts starting to freeze up as the venom courses through you, until you are utterly paralysed. Your heart is filled with horror and dread as you are dragged down into its sandy den to be slowly devoured alive. Perhaps your god will take pity on you.

goto [**476**](#HeadingNum476)

#### 1705

#### 1706

As the sun is setting you go up to the highest balcony of the palace and look out over the city. You've spent many evenings drinking in the sublime beauty of this sight, but you have never felt it more keenly than now. The tropical haze shading from gold into the velvet grey-blue of dusk. The palm trees stirring faintly in the breeze off the bay. The dust, the smells of the river, spices from the market, camel dung, incense from the temples, woodsmoke and jasmine flowers. The clouds of gnats over the reeds beyond the city wall. The murmurs and shouts drifting up from the plaza below. And far out, mere daubs against the horizon, the white-sailed ships painting foam streaks across the darkening sea.

You raise your voice in song. 'Like a tent in the storm, like a cabin battered by winds, my home has been torn up, my treasures scattered...'

The last notes fade. There is stillness and silence, broken at last by the tolling of a single temple bell.

goto [**830**](#HeadingNum830)
